<a href="/wiki/List_of_coats_of_arms_of_Prime_Ministers_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="List of coats of arms of Prime Ministers of the United Kingdom"><img alt="Coat of Arms of Margaret Thatcher, The Baroness Thatcher (1995–2013).svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Coat_of_Arms_of_Margaret_Thatcher%2C_The_Baroness_Thatcher_%281995%E2%80%932013%29.svg/30px-Coat_of_Arms_of_Margaret_Thatcher%2C_The_Baroness_Thatcher_%281995%E2%80%932013%29.svg.png" width="30" height="26" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Coat_of_Arms_of_Margaret_Thatcher%2C_The_Baroness_Thatcher_%281995%E2%80%932013%29.svg/45px-Coat_of_Arms_of_Margaret_Thatcher%2C_The_Baroness_Thatcher_%281995%E2%80%932013%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Coat_of_Arms_of_Margaret_Thatcher%2C_The_Baroness_Thatcher_%281995%E2%80%932013%29.svg/60px-Coat_of_Arms_of_Margaret_Thatcher%2C_The_Baroness_Thatcher_%281995%E2%80%932013%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1470" data-file-height="1297" /></a></td>
</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;text-align:center;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Margaret_Thatcher_sidebar" title="Template:Margaret Thatcher sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Margaret_Thatcher_sidebar" title="Template talk:Margaret Thatcher sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Margaret_Thatcher_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>

<p><b>Margaret Hilda Thatcher, Baroness Thatcher</b>, <span class="noexcerpt" style="font-size:100%;"><a href="/wiki/Lady_of_the_Order_of_the_Garter" class="mw-redirect" title="Lady of the Order of the Garter">LG</a>,&#32;<a href="/wiki/Member_of_the_Order_of_Merit" class="mw-redirect" title="Member of the Order of Merit">OM</a>,&#32;<a href="/wiki/Dame_of_Justice_of_the_Order_of_St_John" class="mw-redirect" title="Dame of Justice of the Order of St John">DStJ</a>,&#32;<a href="/wiki/Privy_Council_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Privy Council of the United Kingdom">PC</a>,&#32;<a href="/wiki/Fellow_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Fellow of the Royal Society">FRS</a>,&#32;<a href="/wiki/Honorary_Fellows_of_the_Royal_Society_of_Chemistry" title="Honorary Fellows of the Royal Society of Chemistry">HonFRSC</a></span> (<a href="/wiki/Given_name#Name_at_birth" title="Given name">née</a>&#160;<b>Roberts</b>; 13 October 1925&#160;&#8211;&#32;8 April 2013) was a British <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/stateswoman" class="extiw" title="wikt:stateswoman">stateswoman</a> who served as <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Prime Minister of the United Kingdom</a> from 1979 to 1990 and <a href="/wiki/Leader_of_the_Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Leader of the Conservative Party (UK)">Leader of the Conservative Party</a> from 1975 to 1990. She was the longest-serving British prime minister of the 20th century and the first woman to hold that office. A Soviet journalist dubbed her the <b>Iron Lady</b>, a nickname that became associated with her uncompromising politics and leadership style. As Prime Minister, she implemented policies known as <a href="/wiki/Thatcherism" title="Thatcherism">Thatcherism</a>.
</p><p>A research <a href="/wiki/Chemist" title="Chemist">chemist</a> at <a href="/wiki/Somerville_College,_Oxford" title="Somerville College, Oxford">Somerville College, Oxford</a>, before becoming a <a href="/wiki/Barrister" title="Barrister">barrister</a>, Thatcher was <a href="/wiki/List_of_MPs_elected_in_the_United_Kingdom_general_election,_1959" title="List of MPs elected in the United Kingdom general election, 1959">elected Member of Parliament</a> for <a href="/wiki/Finchley_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Finchley (UK Parliament constituency)">Finchley</a> in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1959" title="United Kingdom general election, 1959">1959</a>. <a href="/wiki/Edward_Heath" title="Edward Heath">Edward Heath</a> appointed her <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Education" title="Secretary of State for Education">Secretary of State for Education and Science</a> in his <a href="/wiki/Heath_ministry" title="Heath ministry">Conservative government</a>. In 1975, Thatcher defeated Heath in the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)_leadership_election,_1975" title="Conservative Party (UK) leadership election, 1975">Conservative Party leadership election</a> to become <a href="/wiki/Leader_of_the_Opposition_(United_Kingdom)" title="Leader of the Opposition (United Kingdom)">Leader of the Opposition</a>, the first woman to lead a major political party in the United Kingdom. She became Prime Minister after winning the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1979" title="United Kingdom general election, 1979">1979 general election</a>.
</p><p>Thatcher introduced a series of economic policies intended to reverse high unemployment and Britain's struggles in the wake of the <a href="/wiki/Winter_of_Discontent" title="Winter of Discontent">Winter of Discontent</a> and an ongoing recession.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;nb 1&#93;</a></sup> Her political philosophy and economic policies emphasised <a href="/wiki/Deregulation" title="Deregulation">deregulation</a> (particularly of the financial sector), flexible labour markets, the privatisation of <a href="/wiki/State-owned_enterprise" title="State-owned enterprise">state-owned companies</a>, and reducing the power and influence of trade unions. Thatcher's popularity in her first years in office waned <a href="/wiki/Early_1980s_recession" title="Early 1980s recession">amid recession</a> and increasing unemployment, until victory in the 1982 <a href="/wiki/Falklands_War" title="Falklands War">Falklands War</a> and the recovering economy brought a resurgence of support, resulting in her decisive <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1983" title="United Kingdom general election, 1983">re-election in 1983</a>. She survived an assassination attempt in the <a href="/wiki/Brighton_hotel_bombing" title="Brighton hotel bombing">Brighton hotel bombing</a> in 1984.
</p><p>Thatcher was re-elected for a third term in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1987" title="United Kingdom general election, 1987">1987</a>, but her subsequent support for the <a href="/wiki/Poll_tax_(Great_Britain)" title="Poll tax (Great Britain)">Community Charge</a> ("poll tax") was widely unpopular, and her views on the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">European Community</a> were not shared by others in her Cabinet. She resigned as Prime Minister and party leader in November 1990, after <a href="/wiki/Michael_Heseltine" title="Michael Heseltine">Michael Heseltine</a> launched a <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)_leadership_election,_1990" title="Conservative Party (UK) leadership election, 1990">challenge to her leadership</a>. After retiring from the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">Commons</a> in 1992, she was given a <a href="/wiki/Life_peer" title="Life peer">life peerage</a> as Baroness Thatcher (of <a href="/wiki/Kesteven" title="Kesteven">Kesteven</a> in the County of <a href="/wiki/Lincolnshire" title="Lincolnshire">Lincolnshire</a>) which entitled her to sit in the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a>. In 2013, she <a href="/wiki/Death_and_funeral_of_Margaret_Thatcher" title="Death and funeral of Margaret Thatcher">died of a stroke</a> in London at the age of 87.
</p><p>Always a controversial figure, she is nonetheless viewed favorably in <a href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_Prime_Ministers_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Historical rankings of Prime Ministers of the United Kingdom">historical rankings of British prime ministers</a>, and her tenure constituted a <a href="/wiki/Realigning_election" title="Realigning election">realignment</a> towards <a href="/wiki/Neoliberalism" title="Neoliberalism">neoliberal</a> policies in the United Kingdom; despite the passage of time, debate over the complicated legacy of Thatcherism persists.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_education">Early life and education</span></h2>
<p>Margaret Hilda Roberts was born on 13 October 1925, in <a href="/wiki/Grantham,_Lincolnshire" class="mw-redirect" title="Grantham, Lincolnshire">Grantham, Lincolnshire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-whoswho_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-whoswho-3">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Her parents were <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Roberts" title="Alfred Roberts">Alfred Roberts</a> (1892–1970), from Northamptonshire, and Beatrice Ethel (née Stephenson, 1888–1960), from Lincolnshire.<sup id="cite_ref-whoswho_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-whoswho-3">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett20061_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett20061-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> She spent her childhood in Grantham, where her father owned two grocery shops. In 1938, <a href="/wiki/Events_preceding_World_War_II_in_Europe" title="Events preceding World War II in Europe">prior to the Second World War</a>, the Roberts family briefly gave sanctuary to a teenage Jewish girl who had <a href="/wiki/Jews_escaping_from_German-occupied_Europe_to_the_United_Kingdom" title="Jews escaping from German-occupied Europe to the United Kingdom">escaped Nazi Germany</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell201138–39_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell201138–39-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Margaret and her <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/penfriend" class="extiw" title="wikt:penfriend">pen-friending</a> sister Muriel saved <a href="/wiki/Allowance_(money)" title="Allowance (money)">pocket money</a> to help pay for the teenager's journey.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell201138–39_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell201138–39-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div style="clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;background-color:transparent">Thatcher's birthplace</div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Maison_natale_de_Margaret_Thatcher,_Grantham.JPG" class="image"><img alt="The corner of a terraced suburban street. The lower storey is a corner shop, advertising as a chiropractic clinic. The building is two storeys high, with some parts three storeys high." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Maison_natale_de_Margaret_Thatcher%2C_Grantham.JPG/200px-Maison_natale_de_Margaret_Thatcher%2C_Grantham.JPG" width="200" height="94" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Maison_natale_de_Margaret_Thatcher%2C_Grantham.JPG/300px-Maison_natale_de_Margaret_Thatcher%2C_Grantham.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Maison_natale_de_Margaret_Thatcher%2C_Grantham.JPG/400px-Maison_natale_de_Margaret_Thatcher%2C_Grantham.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1214" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">2009 photograph of what was her father's grocery in Grantham.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;nb 2&#93;</a></sup></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Plaque,_maison_natale_de_Margaret_Thatcher.JPG" class="image"><img alt="&quot;Birth place of the Rt.Hon. Margaret Thatcher, M.P. First woman prime minister of Great Britain and Northern Ireland&quot;" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Plaque%2C_maison_natale_de_Margaret_Thatcher.JPG/200px-Plaque%2C_maison_natale_de_Margaret_Thatcher.JPG" width="200" height="111" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Plaque%2C_maison_natale_de_Margaret_Thatcher.JPG/300px-Plaque%2C_maison_natale_de_Margaret_Thatcher.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Plaque%2C_maison_natale_de_Margaret_Thatcher.JPG/400px-Plaque%2C_maison_natale_de_Margaret_Thatcher.JPG 2x" data-file-width="957" data-file-height="532" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Commemorative plaque<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup></div></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><span class="nowrap">Coord. <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Margaret_Thatcher&amp;params=52_54_57.09_N_0_38_42.40_W_"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">52°54′57.09″N</span> <span class="longitude">0°38′42.40″W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">&#xfeff; / &#xfeff;</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">52.9158583°N 0.6451111°W</span><span style="display:none">&#xfeff; / <span class="geo">52.9158583; -0.6451111</span></span></span></a></span></span></div></div></div>
<p>Alfred Roberts was an <a href="/wiki/Alderman" title="Alderman">alderman</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Methodist_local_preacher" title="Methodist local preacher">Methodist local preacher</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett20068_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett20068-9">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> and brought up his daughter as a strict <a href="/wiki/Wesleyan_Methodist_Church_(Great_Britain)" title="Wesleyan Methodist Church (Great Britain)">Wesleyan Methodist</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Johnson_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> attending the <a href="/wiki/Finkin_Street_Methodist_Church" title="Finkin Street Methodist Church">Finkin Street Methodist Church</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> He came from a <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal</a> family but stood (as was then customary in local government) as an <a href="/wiki/Independent_politician" title="Independent politician">Independent</a>. He served as Mayor of Grantham from 1945–46 and lost his position as alderman in 1952 after the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a> won its first majority on Grantham Council in 1950.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett20068_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett20068-9">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Margaret_Thatcher_aos_13_anos_de_idade.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Photograph" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Margaret_Thatcher_aos_13_anos_de_idade.jpg/170px-Margaret_Thatcher_aos_13_anos_de_idade.jpg" width="170" height="245" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Margaret_Thatcher_aos_13_anos_de_idade.jpg/255px-Margaret_Thatcher_aos_13_anos_de_idade.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f6/Margaret_Thatcher_aos_13_anos_de_idade.jpg 2x" data-file-width="333" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Margaret_Thatcher_aos_13_anos_de_idade.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of Margaret Roberts aged 12 or 13</div></div></div>
<p>Margaret Roberts attended <a href="/wiki/Huntingtower_Road_Primary_School" class="mw-redirect" title="Huntingtower Road Primary School">Huntingtower Road Primary School</a> and won a scholarship to <a href="/wiki/Kesteven_and_Grantham_Girls%27_School" title="Kesteven and Grantham Girls&#39; School">Kesteven and Grantham Girls' School</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Grammar_school" title="Grammar school">grammar school</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-whoswho_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-whoswho-3">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett20065_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett20065-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Her school reports showed hard work and continual improvement; her extracurricular activities included the piano, <a href="/wiki/Field_hockey" title="Field hockey">field hockey</a>, poetry recitals, swimming and walking.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett20066Blundell200821–22_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett20066Blundell200821–22-13">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> She was <a href="/wiki/Head_girl_and_head_boy" title="Head girl and head boy">head girl</a> in 1942–43.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> In her <a href="/wiki/Sixth_form" title="Sixth form">upper sixth year</a> she applied for a scholarship to study chemistry at <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">University of Oxford</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Somerville_College,_Oxford" title="Somerville College, Oxford">Somerville College</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_college" title="Women&#39;s college">women's college</a> at the time, but she was initially rejected and was offered a place only after another candidate withdrew.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett200612Blundell200823_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett200612Blundell200823-15">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Roberts arrived at Oxford in 1943 and graduated in 1947<sup id="cite_ref-whoswho_3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-whoswho-3">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> with <a href="/wiki/British_undergraduate_degree_classification" title="British undergraduate degree classification">Second-Class Honours</a>, in the four-year Chemistry <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Science" title="Bachelor of Science">Bachelor of Science</a> degree, specialising in <a href="/wiki/X-ray_crystallography" title="X-ray crystallography">X-ray crystallography</a> under the supervision of <a href="/wiki/Dorothy_Hodgkin" title="Dorothy Hodgkin">Dorothy Hodgkin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundell200825–27Beckett200616_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundell200825–27Beckett200616-16">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Her dissertation was on the structure of the antibiotic <a href="/wiki/Gramicidin" title="Gramicidin">gramicidin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell200065_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell200065-17">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Thatcher did not devote herself entirely to studying chemistry as she only intended to be a chemist for a short period of time.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell200047_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell200047-18">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Even when working on the subject, she was already thinking towards law and politics.<sup id="cite_ref-lecher20130408_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lecher20130408-19">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> She was reportedly more proud of becoming the first Prime Minister with a science degree than the first female Prime Minister,<sup id="cite_ref-runciman20130606_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-runciman20130606-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> and as Prime Minister attempted to preserve Somerville as a women's college.<sup id="cite_ref-bowcott20161230_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bowcott20161230-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During her time at Oxford, she was noted for her isolated and serious attitude.<sup id="cite_ref-Oxford1_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford1-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Her first boyfriend, <span id="Tony_Bray">Tony Bray</span> (1926–2014), recalled that she was "very thoughtful and a very good conversationalist. That's probably what interested me. She was good at general subjects".<sup id="cite_ref-Oxford1_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford1-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bray_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bray-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Her enthusiasm for politics as a girl made him think of her as "unusual".<sup id="cite_ref-Oxford1_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford1-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Bray met Roberts' parents and described her father as "slightly austere" and her mother as "very proper".<sup id="cite_ref-Oxford1_22-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford1-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bray_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bray-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At the end of the term at Oxford, Bray gradually became more distant and hoped for their relationship to "fizzle out". Bray later recalled that he thought Roberts had taken the relationship more seriously than he had done.<sup id="cite_ref-Oxford1_22-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford1-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> When asked about Bray in later life, Thatcher prevaricated but acknowledged the circumstances between herself and Bray.<sup id="cite_ref-Oxford1_22-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford1-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bray_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bray-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Roberts became President of the <a href="/wiki/Oxford_University_Conservative_Association" title="Oxford University Conservative Association">Oxford University Conservative Association</a> in 1946.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett200620–21Blundell200828_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett200620–21Blundell200828-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> She was influenced at university by political works such as <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Hayek" title="Friedrich Hayek">Friedrich Hayek</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Road_to_Serfdom" title="The Road to Serfdom">The Road to Serfdom</a></i> (1944),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundell200830_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundell200830-25">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> which condemned economic intervention by government as a precursor to an authoritarian state.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReitan200317_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReitan200317-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Postgraduate_career:_1947.E2.80.931951"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Postgraduate_career:_1947–1951">Postgraduate career: 1947–1951</span></h3>
<table class="rquote pullquote floatright" role="presentation" style="border-collapse:collapse; border-style:none; float:right; margin:0.5em 0.75em; width:22em;">

<tbody><tr style="text-align:left; vertical-align:top;">
<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:&#39;Times New Roman&#39;,serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">“
</td>
<td style="padding:0 10px;">This woman is headstrong, obstinate and dangerously self-opinionated.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC2013_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC2013-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</td>
<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:&#39;Times New Roman&#39;,serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; text-align:right; vertical-align:bottom; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">”
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td class="rquotecite" colspan="3" style="padding-top:10px; font-size:smaller; line-height:normal; text-align:right;"><cite>—&#160;&#32;Assessment of Thatcher by the ICI in 1948</cite>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>After graduating, Roberts moved to <a href="/wiki/Colchester" title="Colchester">Colchester</a> in Essex to work as a research chemist for <a href="/wiki/BX_Plastics" title="BX Plastics">BX Plastics</a> near <a href="/wiki/Manningtree" title="Manningtree">Manningtree</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett200617_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett200617-28">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> In 1948 she applied for a job at <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Chemical_Industries" title="Imperial Chemical Industries">Imperial Chemical Industries</a> (ICI), but was rejected after the personnel department assessed her as "headstrong, obstinate and dangerously self-opinionated".<sup id="cite_ref-BBC2013_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC2013-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Professor Jon Agar argued that her understanding of modern scientific research impacted her views as Prime Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAgar2011_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAgar2011-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Roberts joined the local Conservative Association and attended the party conference at <a href="/wiki/Llandudno,_Wales" class="mw-redirect" title="Llandudno, Wales">Llandudno, Wales</a>, in 1948, as a representative of the University Graduate Conservative Association.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett200622_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett200622-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Meanwhile, she became a high-ranking affiliate of the <a href="/wiki/Vermin_Club" title="Vermin Club">Vermin Club</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> a group of grassroots Conservatives formed in response to a derogatory comment made by <a href="/wiki/Aneurin_Bevan" title="Aneurin Bevan">Aneurin Bevan</a>. One of her Oxford friends was also a friend of the Chair of the <a href="/wiki/Dartford" title="Dartford">Dartford</a> Conservative Association in <a href="/wiki/Kent" title="Kent">Kent</a>, who were looking for candidates.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett200622_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett200622-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Officials of the association were so impressed by her that they asked her to apply, even though she was not on the party's approved list; she was selected in January 1950 (aged 24) and added to the approved list <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/post_ante" class="extiw" title="wikt:post ante"><i>post ante</i></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundell200836_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundell200836-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At a dinner following her formal adoption as Conservative candidate for Dartford in February 1949 she met <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/divorc%C3%A9#Latin" class="extiw" title="wikt:divorcé">divorcé</a> <a href="/wiki/Denis_Thatcher" title="Denis Thatcher">Denis Thatcher</a>, a successful and wealthy businessman, who drove her to her Essex train.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett200622Blundell200836_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett200622Blundell200836-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> After their first meeting she described him to Muriel as "not a very attractive creature&#160;&#8211;&#32;very reserved but quite nice".<sup id="cite_ref-Oxford1_22-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford1-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> In preparation for the election Roberts moved to Dartford, where she supported herself by working as a research chemist for <a href="/wiki/J._Lyons_and_Co." title="J. Lyons and Co.">J. Lyons and Co.</a> in Hammersmith, part of a team developing <a href="/wiki/Emulsion" title="Emulsion">emulsifiers</a> for <a href="/wiki/Ice_cream" title="Ice cream">ice cream</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett200622&#39;&#39;New_Scientist&#39;&#39;1983_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett200622&#39;&#39;New_Scientist&#39;&#39;1983-35">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Shortly after her marriage to Denis, she and her husband began attending <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Anglican</a> services and would later convert to <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglicanism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Belz_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Belz-36">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Filby_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Filby-37">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_political_career">Early political career</span></h2>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1950" title="United Kingdom general election, 1950">1950</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1951" title="United Kingdom general election, 1951">1951 general elections</a>, Roberts was the Conservative candidate for the safe Labour seat of <a href="/wiki/Dartford_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Dartford (UK Parliament constituency)">Dartford</a>. The local party selected her as its candidate because, though not a dynamic public speaker, Roberts was well-prepared and fearless in her answers; prospective candidate <a href="/wiki/Bill_Deedes" title="Bill Deedes">Bill Deedes</a> recalled that "Once she opened her mouth, the rest of us began to look rather second-rate".<sup id="cite_ref-runciman20130606_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-runciman20130606-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> She attracted media attention as the youngest and the only female candidate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett200623–24Blundell200837_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett200623–24Blundell200837-38">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> She lost on both occasions to <a href="/wiki/Norman_Dodds" title="Norman Dodds">Norman Dodds</a>, but reduced the Labour majority by 6,000, and then a further 1,000.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett200623–24_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett200623–24-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> During the campaigns, she was supported by her parents and by future husband Denis Thatcher, whom she married in December 1951.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett200623–24_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett200623–24-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Denis_Thatcher_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Denis_Thatcher-40">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Denis funded his wife's studies for the <a href="/wiki/Bar_association" title="Bar association">bar</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett200625_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett200625-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> she qualified as a barrister in 1953 and specialised in taxation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundell200835_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundell200835-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Later that same year their twins <a href="/wiki/Carol_Thatcher" title="Carol Thatcher">Carol</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mark_Thatcher" title="Mark Thatcher">Mark</a> were born, delivered prematurely by <a href="/wiki/Caesarean_section" title="Caesarean section">Caesarean section</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOgden199070Beckett200626Aitken201374_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOgden199070Beckett200626Aitken201374-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Member_of_Parliament:_1959.E2.80.931970"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Member_of_Parliament:_1959–1970">Member of Parliament: 1959–1970</span></h3>
<p>In 1954, Thatcher was defeated when she sought selection to be the Conservative party candidate for the <a href="/wiki/Orpington_by-election,_1955" title="Orpington by-election, 1955">Orpington by-election</a> of January 1955. She chose not to stand as a candidate in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1955" title="United Kingdom general election, 1955">1955 general election</a>, in later years stating: "I really just felt the twins were&#160;... only two, I really felt that it was too soon. I couldn't do that."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell2000100_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell2000100-44">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Afterwards, Thatcher began looking for a Conservative <a href="/wiki/Safe_seat" title="Safe seat">safe seat</a> and was selected as the candidate for <a href="/wiki/Finchley_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Finchley (UK Parliament constituency)">Finchley</a> in April 1958 (narrowly beating <a href="/wiki/Ian_Fraser_(Plymouth_Sutton_MP)" title="Ian Fraser (Plymouth Sutton MP)">Ian Montagu Fraser</a>). She was elected as MP for the seat after a hard campaign in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1959" title="United Kingdom general election, 1959">1959 election</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett200627_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett200627-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Benefiting from her fortunate result in a lottery for <a href="/wiki/Backbencher" title="Backbencher">backbenchers</a> to propose new legislation,<sup id="cite_ref-runciman20130606_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-runciman20130606-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Thatcher's <a href="/wiki/Maiden_speech" title="Maiden speech">maiden speech</a> was, unusually, in support of her <a href="/wiki/Private_member%27s_bill" title="Private member&#39;s bill">private member's bill</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Public_Bodies_(Admission_to_Meetings)_Act_1960" title="Public Bodies (Admission to Meetings) Act 1960">Public Bodies (Admission to Meetings) Act 1960</a>), requiring local authorities to hold their council meetings in public; the bill was successful and became law.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAitken201391_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAitken201391-48">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> In 1961 she went against the Conservative Party's official position by voting for the restoration of <a href="/wiki/Birching" title="Birching">birching</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Judicial_corporal_punishment" title="Judicial corporal punishment">judicial corporal punishment</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell2000134_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell2000134-49">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thatcher's talent and drive caused her to be mentioned as a future Prime Minister in her early 20s<sup id="cite_ref-runciman20130606_20-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-runciman20130606-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> although she herself was more pessimistic, stating as late as 1970: "There will not be a woman prime minister in my lifetime&#160;&#8211;&#32;the male population is too prejudiced."<sup id="cite_ref-sandbrook20130409_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sandbrook20130409-50">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> In October 1961 she was promoted to the <a href="/wiki/Frontbench" class="mw-redirect" title="Frontbench">frontbench</a> as <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_Secretary_to_the_Minister_for_Pensions" title="Parliamentary Secretary to the Minister for Pensions">Parliamentary Undersecretary at the Ministry of Pensions and National Insurance</a> by <a href="/wiki/Harold_Macmillan" title="Harold Macmillan">Harold Macmillan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReitan20034_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReitan20034-51">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Thatcher was the youngest woman in history to receive such a post, and among the first <a href="/wiki/List_of_MPs_elected_in_the_United_Kingdom_general_election,_1959" title="List of MPs elected in the United Kingdom general election, 1959">MPs elected in 1959</a> to be promoted.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott-Smith2003_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott-Smith2003-52">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> After the Conservatives lost the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1964" title="United Kingdom general election, 1964">1964 election</a> she became spokesman on Housing and Land, in which position she advocated her party's policy of allowing tenants to buy their <a href="/wiki/Council_house" title="Council house">council houses</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWapshott200764_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWapshott200764-53">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> She moved to the Shadow <a href="/wiki/HM_Treasury" title="HM Treasury">Treasury</a> team in 1966 and, as Treasury spokesman, opposed Labour's mandatory price and income controls, arguing they would unintentionally produce effects that would distort the economy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWapshott200764_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWapshott200764-53">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By 1966, party leaders viewed Thatcher as a potential <a href="/wiki/Official_Opposition_Shadow_Cabinet_(United_Kingdom)" title="Official Opposition Shadow Cabinet (United Kingdom)">Shadow Cabinet member</a>. <a href="/wiki/Jim_Prior" title="Jim Prior">Jim Prior</a> suggested her as a member after the Conservatives' <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1966" title="United Kingdom general election, 1966">1966 defeat</a>, but party leader <a href="/wiki/Edward_Heath" title="Edward Heath">Edward Heath</a> and Chief Whip <a href="/wiki/William_Whitelaw,_1st_Viscount_Whitelaw" title="William Whitelaw, 1st Viscount Whitelaw">William Whitelaw</a> eventually settled on <a href="/wiki/Mervyn_Pike,_Baroness_Pike" title="Mervyn Pike, Baroness Pike">Mervyn Pike</a> as the <a href="/wiki/First_Shadow_Cabinet_of_Edward_Heath" title="First Shadow Cabinet of Edward Heath">Shadow Cabinet</a>'s sole woman member.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott-Smith2003_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott-Smith2003-52">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At the 1966 Conservative Party conference she criticised the high-tax policies of the <a href="/wiki/Labour_government,_1964%E2%80%931970" title="Labour government, 1964–1970">Labour government</a> as being steps "not only towards Socialism, but towards Communism", arguing that lower taxes served as an incentive to hard work.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWapshott200764_53-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWapshott200764-53">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Thatcher was one of the few Conservative MPs to support <a href="/wiki/Leo_Abse" title="Leo Abse">Leo Abse</a>'s bill to decriminalise male homosexuality.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> She voted in favour of <a href="/wiki/David_Steel" title="David Steel">David Steel</a>'s bill to legalise abortion,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThatcher1995150_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThatcher1995150-55">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> as well as a ban on <a href="/wiki/Hare_coursing" title="Hare coursing">hare coursing</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> She supported the retention of capital punishment<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> and voted against the relaxation of divorce laws.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThatcher1995151_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThatcher1995151-60">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1967, the <a href="/wiki/Embassy_of_the_United_States,_London" title="Embassy of the United States, London">United States Embassy in London</a> chose Thatcher to take part in the <a href="/wiki/International_Visitor_Leadership_Program" title="International Visitor Leadership Program">International Visitor Leadership Program</a> (then called the Foreign Leader Program), a professional exchange programme that gave her the opportunity to spend about six weeks visiting various US cities and political figures as well as institutions such as the <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a>. Although she was not yet a Shadow Cabinet member, the embassy reportedly described her to the State Department as a possible future Prime Minister. The description helped Thatcher meet with prominent persons during a busy itinerary focused on economic issues, including <a href="/wiki/Paul_Samuelson" title="Paul Samuelson">Paul Samuelson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Walt_Whitman_Rostow" title="Walt Whitman Rostow">Walt Rostow</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pierre-Paul_Schweitzer" title="Pierre-Paul Schweitzer">Pierre-Paul Schweitzer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Rockefeller" title="Nelson Rockefeller">Nelson Rockefeller</a>. Following the visit, Heath appointed Thatcher to the Shadow Cabinet<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott-Smith2003_52-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott-Smith2003-52">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> as Fuel and Power spokesman.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Prior to the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1970" title="United Kingdom general election, 1970">1970 general election</a>, she was promoted to Shadow Transport spokesman and later to Education.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWapshott200765_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWapshott200765-62">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1968 <a href="/wiki/Enoch_Powell" title="Enoch Powell">Enoch Powell</a> delivered his <a href="/wiki/Rivers_of_Blood_speech" title="Rivers of Blood speech">"Rivers of Blood" speech</a> in which he strongly criticised Commonwealth <a href="/wiki/Modern_immigration_to_the_United_Kingdom" title="Modern immigration to the United Kingdom">immigration to the United Kingdom</a> and the then-proposed <a href="/wiki/Race_Relations_Act_1968" title="Race Relations Act 1968">Race Relations Bill</a>. When Heath telephoned Thatcher to inform her that he was going to sack Powell from the Shadow Cabinet, she recalled that she "really thought that it was better to let things cool down for the present rather than heighten the crisis". She believed that his main points about Commonwealth immigration were correct and that his selected quotations from his speech had been taken out of context.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAitken2013117_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAitken2013117-63">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> In a 1991 interview for <i><a href="/wiki/Today_(UK_newspaper)" title="Today (UK newspaper)">Today</a></i>, Thatcher stated that she thought Powell had "made a valid argument, if in sometimes regrettable terms".<sup id="cite_ref-sandford_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sandford-64">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Around this time she gave her first Commons speech as a Shadow Transport minister and highlighted the need for investment in <a href="/wiki/British_Rail" title="British Rail">British Rail</a>. She argued: "...&#160;if we build bigger and better roads, they would soon be saturated with more vehicles and we would be no nearer solving the problem."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell2000189_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell2000189-65">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Thatcher made her first visit to the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> in the summer of 1969 as the Opposition Transport spokeswoman,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell2000190_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell2000190-66">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> and in October delivered a speech celebrating her ten years in Parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell2000191_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell2000191-67">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> A couple of months later, in early 1970, she told <i>The Finchley Press</i> that she would like to see a "reversal of the permissive society".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell2000191_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell2000191-67">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Education_Secretary:_1970.E2.80.931974"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Education_Secretary:_1970–1974">Education Secretary: 1970–1974</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Girls_at_Baldock_County_Council_School_in_Hertfordshire_enjoy_a_drink_of_milk_during_a_break_in_the_school_day_in_1944._D20552.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Photograph" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Girls_at_Baldock_County_Council_School_in_Hertfordshire_enjoy_a_drink_of_milk_during_a_break_in_the_school_day_in_1944._D20552.jpg/170px-Girls_at_Baldock_County_Council_School_in_Hertfordshire_enjoy_a_drink_of_milk_during_a_break_in_the_school_day_in_1944._D20552.jpg" width="170" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Girls_at_Baldock_County_Council_School_in_Hertfordshire_enjoy_a_drink_of_milk_during_a_break_in_the_school_day_in_1944._D20552.jpg/255px-Girls_at_Baldock_County_Council_School_in_Hertfordshire_enjoy_a_drink_of_milk_during_a_break_in_the_school_day_in_1944._D20552.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Girls_at_Baldock_County_Council_School_in_Hertfordshire_enjoy_a_drink_of_milk_during_a_break_in_the_school_day_in_1944._D20552.jpg/340px-Girls_at_Baldock_County_Council_School_in_Hertfordshire_enjoy_a_drink_of_milk_during_a_break_in_the_school_day_in_1944._D20552.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4546" data-file-height="4447" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Girls_at_Baldock_County_Council_School_in_Hertfordshire_enjoy_a_drink_of_milk_during_a_break_in_the_school_day_in_1944._D20552.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thatcher abolished free milk for some primary school children <i>(pictured)</i> in 1970 as the Labour Party had done for secondary schools in 1968</div></div></div>
<p>The Conservative Party led by <a href="/wiki/Edward_Heath" title="Edward Heath">Edward Heath</a> won the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1970" title="United Kingdom general election, 1970">1970 general election</a>, and Thatcher was subsequently appointed to the <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Cabinet of the United Kingdom">Cabinet</a> as <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Education" title="Secretary of State for Education">Secretary of State for Education and Science</a>. Thatcher caused controversy when after only a few days in office she withdrew Labour's <a href="/wiki/Circular_10/65" title="Circular 10/65">Circular 10/65</a> which attempted to force comprehensivisation, without going through a consultation progress.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell2000222_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell2000222-68">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> She was highly criticized for the speed in which she carried this out.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell2000222_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell2000222-68">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Consequently, she drafted her own new policy (<a href="/wiki/Circular_10/70" title="Circular 10/70">Circular 10/70</a>) which ensured that a local authority was not forced to go comprehensive.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore2013215_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore2013215-69">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Her new policy was not meant to stop the development of new comprehensives; she said: "We shall&#160;... expect plans to be based on educational considerations rather than on the comprehensive principle."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore2013215_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore2013215-69">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thatcher supported <a href="/wiki/Victor_Rothschild,_3rd_Baron_Rothschild" title="Victor Rothschild, 3rd Baron Rothschild">Lord Rothschild</a>'s 1971 proposal for market forces to affect government funding of research. Although many scientists opposed the proposal, her research background probably made her sceptical of their claim that outsiders should not interfere with funding.<sup id="cite_ref-lecher20130408_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lecher20130408-19">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The department evaluated proposals for more local education authorities to close <a href="/wiki/Grammar_school" title="Grammar school">grammar schools</a> and to adopt <a href="/wiki/Comprehensive_school" title="Comprehensive school">comprehensive secondary education</a>. Although Thatcher was committed to a tiered <a href="/wiki/Secondary_modern_school" title="Secondary modern school">secondary modern</a>-grammar school system of education and attempted to preserve grammar schools,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReitan200314_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReitan200314-70">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> during her tenure as Education Secretary she turned down only 326 of 3,612&#160;proposals (roughly 9 per cent)<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell2000224_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell2000224-71">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> for schools to become comprehensives; the proportion of pupils attending comprehensive schools consequently rose from 32&#160;per&#160;cent to 62&#160;per&#160;cent.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarr2007248–249_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarr2007248–249-72">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, she managed to save 94 grammar schools.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore2013215_69-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore2013215-69">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><span id="Milk_Snatcher"></span>
During her first months in office she attracted public attention as a consequence of the government's attempts to cut spending. She gave priority to academic needs in schools,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReitan200314_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReitan200314-70">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> while administering public expenditure cuts on the state education system, resulting in the abolition of <a href="/wiki/Education_Act_1944" title="Education Act 1944">free milk for schoolchildren</a> aged seven to eleven.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWapshott200776_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWapshott200776-73">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> She held that few children would suffer if schools were charged for milk, but agreed to provide younger children with ⅓ pint daily for nutritional purposes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWapshott200776_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWapshott200776-73">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> She also argued that she was simply carrying on with what the Labour government had started since they had stopped giving free milk to secondary schools.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell2000231_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell2000231-74">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Milk would still be provided to those children that required it on medical grounds and schools could still sell milk.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell2000231_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell2000231-74">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> The aftermath of the milk row hardened her determination, she told the editor-proprietor Harold Creighton of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Spectator" title="The Spectator">The Spectator</a></i>: "Don't underestimate me, I saw how they broke <a href="/wiki/Keith_Joseph" title="Keith Joseph">Keith</a>, but they won't break me."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell2000288_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell2000288-75">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Cabinet papers later revealed that she opposed the policy but had been forced into it by the Treasury.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Her decision provoked a storm of protest from Labour and the press,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReitan200315_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReitan200315-77">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> leading to her being notoriously nicknamed "Margaret Thatcher, Milk Snatcher".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWapshott200776_73-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWapshott200776-73">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> She reportedly considered leaving politics in the aftermath and later wrote in her autobiography: "I learned a valuable lesson &#91;from the experience&#93;. I had incurred the maximum of political odium for the minimum of political benefit."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReitan200315Thatcher1995182_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReitan200315Thatcher1995182-79">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Leader_of_the_Opposition:_1975.E2.80.931979"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Leader_of_the_Opposition:_1975–1979">Leader of the Opposition: 1975–1979</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Shadow_Cabinet_of_Margaret_Thatcher" title="Shadow Cabinet of Margaret Thatcher">Shadow Cabinet of Margaret Thatcher</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Heath_ministry" title="Heath ministry">Heath ministry</a> continued to experience difficulties with <a href="/wiki/1973_oil_crisis" title="1973 oil crisis">oil embargoes</a> and union demands for wage increases in 1973, subsequently losing the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_February_1974" title="United Kingdom general election, February 1974">February 1974 general election</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReitan200315_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReitan200315-77">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Labour formed a <a href="/wiki/Labour_government,_1974%E2%80%931979" title="Labour government, 1974–1979">minority government</a> and went on to win a narrow majority in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_October_1974" title="United Kingdom general election, October 1974">October 1974 general election</a>. Heath's leadership of the Conservative Party looked increasingly in doubt. Thatcher was not initially seen as the obvious replacement, but she eventually became the main challenger, promising a fresh start.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReitan200316_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReitan200316-80">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Her main support came from the parliamentary <a href="/wiki/1922_Committee" title="1922 Committee">1922 Committee</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReitan200316_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReitan200316-80">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Spectator" title="The Spectator">The Spectator</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> but Thatcher's time in office gave her the reputation of a pragmatist rather than that of an ideologue.<sup id="cite_ref-runciman20130606_20-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-runciman20130606-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> She <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)_leadership_election,_1975" title="Conservative Party (UK) leadership election, 1975">defeated Heath</a> on the first ballot and he resigned the leadership.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> In the second ballot she defeated Whitelaw, Heath's preferred successor. Thatcher's election had a polarising effect on the party, as her support was stronger among MPs on the right, and also among those from southern England, and those who had not attended public schools or <a href="/wiki/Oxbridge" title="Oxbridge">Oxbridge</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECowleyBailey2000_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECowleyBailey2000-83">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em;float: right; clear: right; margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em"><caption>External audio</caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-style: italic">
Speech to the <a href="/wiki/National_Press_Club_(United_States)" title="National Press Club (United States)">National Press Club</a></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<a href="/wiki/File:Margaret_Thatcher_at_White_House.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Margaret Thatcher at White House.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Margaret_Thatcher_at_White_House.jpg/220px-Margaret_Thatcher_at_White_House.jpg" width="220" height="190" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Margaret_Thatcher_at_White_House.jpg/330px-Margaret_Thatcher_at_White_House.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Margaret_Thatcher_at_White_House.jpg/440px-Margaret_Thatcher_at_White_House.jpg 2x" data-file-width="594" data-file-height="512" /></a><div>Thatcher at the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a> in 1977</div></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;text-align: left">
<img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nuvola_apps_arts.svg/16px-Nuvola_apps_arts.svg.png" width="16" height="16" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nuvola_apps_arts.svg/24px-Nuvola_apps_arts.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nuvola_apps_arts.svg/32px-Nuvola_apps_arts.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="128" data-file-height="128" /> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.loc.gov/rr/record/pressclub/thatcher.html">Thatcher's speech on 19 September 1975</a> (starts at 7:39, finishes at 28:33)<sup id="cite_ref-npc_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-npc-84">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Thatcher became Conservative Party leader and <a href="/wiki/Leader_of_the_Opposition_(United_Kingdom)" title="Leader of the Opposition (United Kingdom)">Leader of the Opposition</a> on 11 February 1975;<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> she appointed Whitelaw as her <a href="/wiki/Deputy_Leader_of_the_Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Deputy Leader of the Conservative Party (UK)">deputy</a>. Heath was never reconciled to Thatcher's leadership of the party.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore2013394–395,_430_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore2013394–395,_430-87">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Television critic <a href="/wiki/Clive_James" title="Clive James">Clive James</a>, writing in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Observer" title="The Observer">The Observer</a></i> two days prior to the second leadership ballot, compared her voice of 1973 to a cat sliding down a blackboard.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;nb 3&#93;</a></sup> Thatcher had already begun to work on her presentation on the advice of <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Reece" title="Gordon Reece">Gordon Reece</a>, a former television producer. By chance, Reece met the actor <a href="/wiki/Laurence_Olivier" title="Laurence Olivier">Laurence Olivier</a>, who arranged lessons with the <a href="/wiki/Royal_National_Theatre" title="Royal National Theatre">National Theatre</a>'s voice coach.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThatcher1995267_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThatcher1995267-91">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Moore_Vanity_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moore_Vanity-92">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;nb 4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thatcher began attending lunches regularly at the <a href="/wiki/Institute_of_Economic_Affairs" title="Institute of Economic Affairs">Institute of Economic Affairs</a> (IEA), a think tank founded by <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Hayek" title="Friedrich Hayek">Hayekian</a> poultry magnate <a href="/wiki/Antony_Fisher" title="Antony Fisher">Antony Fisher</a>; she had been visiting the IEA and reading its publications since the early 1960s. There she was influenced by the ideas of <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Harris,_Baron_Harris_of_High_Cross" title="Ralph Harris, Baron Harris of High Cross">Ralph Harris</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Seldon" title="Arthur Seldon">Arthur Seldon</a>, and became the face of the ideological movement opposing the <a href="/wiki/Welfare_state_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Welfare state in the United Kingdom">British welfare state</a>. <a href="/wiki/Keynesian_economics" title="Keynesian economics">Keynesian economics</a>, they believed, was weakening Britain. The institute's pamphlets proposed less government, lower taxes, and more freedom for business and consumers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett2010chpt._11_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett2010chpt._11-96">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Shah_and_Margaret_Thatcher.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Shah_and_Margaret_Thatcher.jpg/170px-Shah_and_Margaret_Thatcher.jpg" width="170" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Shah_and_Margaret_Thatcher.jpg/255px-Shah_and_Margaret_Thatcher.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Shah_and_Margaret_Thatcher.jpg/340px-Shah_and_Margaret_Thatcher.jpg 2x" data-file-width="438" data-file-height="370" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Shah_and_Margaret_Thatcher.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thatcher meeting Shah Mohammad Reza Pahlavi in the <a href="/wiki/Niavaran_Complex" title="Niavaran Complex">Niavaran Complex</a>, 1978</div></div></div>
<p>Thatcher intended to promote <a href="/wiki/Neoliberal" class="mw-redirect" title="Neoliberal">neoliberal</a> economic ideas at home and abroad. Despite setting the direction of her foreign policy for a Conservative government, Thatcher was distressed by her repeated failure to shine in the House of Commons. Consequently, Thatcher decided that as "her voice was carrying little weight at home", she would "be heard in the wider world".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell2000344_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell2000344-97">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Thatcher undertook visits across the Atlantic, establishing an international profile and promoting her economic and foreign policies. She toured the United States in 1975, and visited again in 1977, when she met US President <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper201025–26_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper201025–26-98">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Among other foreign trips, she met Shah <a href="/wiki/Mohammad_Reza_Pahlavi" title="Mohammad Reza Pahlavi">Mohammad Reza Pahlavi</a> during a visit to Iran in April 1978.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Thatcher chose to travel without her Shadow Foreign Secretary, <a href="/wiki/Reginald_Maudling" title="Reginald Maudling">Reginald Maudling</a>, in an attempt to make a bolder personal impact.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper201025–26_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper201025–26-98">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In domestic affairs, Thatcher opposed <a href="/wiki/History_of_Scottish_devolution" title="History of Scottish devolution">Scottish devolution (home rule)</a> and the creation of a <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Assembly" title="Scottish Assembly">Scottish Assembly</a>. She instructed Conservative MPs to vote against the Scotland and Wales Bill in December 1976, which was successfully defeated, and then when new Bills were proposed she supported amending the legislation to allow the English to vote in the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_devolution_referendum,_1979" title="Scottish devolution referendum, 1979">1979 referendum</a> on Scottish devolution.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Britain's economy during the 1970s was so weak that Foreign Secretary <a href="/wiki/James_Callaghan" title="James Callaghan">James Callaghan</a> warned his fellow Labour Cabinet members in 1974 of the possibility of "a breakdown of democracy", telling them: "If I were a young man, I would emigrate."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett2010chpt._7_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett2010chpt._7-101">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> In mid-1978, the economy began to recover and opinion polls showed Labour in the lead, with a general election being expected later that year and a Labour win a serious possibility. Now Prime Minister, Callaghan surprised many by announcing on 7 September that there would be no general election that year and he would wait until 1979 before going to the polls. Thatcher reacted to this by branding the Labour government "chickens", and <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal Party</a> leader <a href="/wiki/David_Steel" title="David Steel">David Steel</a> joined in, criticising Labour for "running scared".<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Labour government then faced fresh public unease about the direction of the country and a damaging series of strikes during the winter of 1978–79, dubbed the "<a href="/wiki/Winter_of_Discontent" title="Winter of Discontent">Winter of Discontent</a>". The Conservatives attacked the Labour government's unemployment record, using advertising with the slogan "<a href="/wiki/Labour_Isn%27t_Working" title="Labour Isn&#39;t Working">Labour Isn't Working</a>". A <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1979" title="United Kingdom general election, 1979">general election</a> was called after the Callaghan ministry <a href="/wiki/1979_vote_of_no_confidence_in_the_Callaghan_ministry" title="1979 vote of no confidence in the Callaghan ministry">lost a motion of no confidence</a> in early 1979. The Conservatives won a 44-seat majority in the House of Commons and Thatcher became the first female British prime minister.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButlerKavanagh1980199_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButlerKavanagh1980199-103">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Becoming_the_Iron_Lady">Becoming the Iron Lady</span></h4>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>I stand before you tonight in my Red Star chiffon evening gown, my face softly made up and my fair hair gently waved, the Iron Lady of the Western world.<sup id="cite_ref-Iron_Lady_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Iron_Lady-104">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup></p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>Thatcher embracing her Soviet nickname in 1976</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>In 1976, Thatcher gave her "Britain Awake" foreign policy speech which lambasted the Soviet Union for seeking world dominance.<sup id="cite_ref-britain-awake_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-britain-awake-105">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> The Soviet Army journal <i><a href="/wiki/Krasnaya_Zvezda" title="Krasnaya Zvezda">Krasnaya Zvezda</a></i> (<i>Red Star</i>) rebutted her stance in a piece entitled "Iron Lady Raises Fears" by Captain Yuri Gavrilov<sup id="cite_ref-gavrilov_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gavrilov-106">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> (alluding to "Iron Chancellor" <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Bismarck" title="Otto von Bismarck">Bismarck</a> of imperial Germany). <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sunday_Times" title="The Sunday Times">The Sunday Times</a></i> covered the <i>Red Star</i> article the next day,<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> and Thatcher embraced the <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/epithet" class="extiw" title="wikt:epithet">epithet</a> a week later; in a speech to Finchley Conservatives she compared it to the <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Wellington" class="mw-redirect" title="Duke of Wellington">Duke of Wellington</a>'s nickname "The Iron Duke".<sup id="cite_ref-Iron_Lady_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Iron_Lady-104">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Iron_(metaphor)" title="Iron (metaphor)">metaphorical sobriquet</a> followed her throughout her political career,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAtkinson1984115Kaplan200060_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAtkinson1984115Kaplan200060-108">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> and has since become a generic descriptor for strong-willed female politicians.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Premiership_of_the_United_Kingdom:_1979.E2.80.931990"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Premiership_of_the_United_Kingdom:_1979–1990">Premiership of the United Kingdom: 1979–1990</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Premiership_of_Margaret_Thatcher" title="Premiership of Margaret Thatcher">Premiership of Margaret Thatcher</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/First_Thatcher_ministry" title="First Thatcher ministry">First Thatcher ministry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Second_Thatcher_ministry" title="Second Thatcher ministry">Second Thatcher ministry</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Third_Thatcher_ministry" title="Third Thatcher ministry">Third Thatcher ministry</a></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Margaret_Thatcher_at_Cabinet_table_in_the_White_House.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Margaret_Thatcher_at_Cabinet_table_in_the_White_House.jpg/200px-Margaret_Thatcher_at_Cabinet_table_in_the_White_House.jpg" width="200" height="132" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Margaret_Thatcher_at_Cabinet_table_in_the_White_House.jpg/300px-Margaret_Thatcher_at_Cabinet_table_in_the_White_House.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Margaret_Thatcher_at_Cabinet_table_in_the_White_House.jpg/400px-Margaret_Thatcher_at_Cabinet_table_in_the_White_House.jpg 2x" data-file-width="995" data-file-height="657" /></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Reagan-Thatcher_cabinet_talks.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Thatcher is the only woman in a room, where a dozen men in suits sit around an oval table. Reagan and Thatcher sit opposite each other in the middle of the long axis of the table. The room is decorated in white, with drapes, a gold chandelier and a portrait of Lincoln." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Reagan-Thatcher_cabinet_talks.jpg/200px-Reagan-Thatcher_cabinet_talks.jpg" width="200" height="133" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Reagan-Thatcher_cabinet_talks.jpg/300px-Reagan-Thatcher_cabinet_talks.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Reagan-Thatcher_cabinet_talks.jpg/400px-Reagan-Thatcher_cabinet_talks.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2033" data-file-height="1350" /></a></div></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Thatcher in the <a href="/wiki/White_House_Cabinet_Room" class="mw-redirect" title="White House Cabinet Room">White House Cabinet Room</a> leading a discussion with the cabinets of <a href="/wiki/Carter_cabinet" class="mw-redirect" title="Carter cabinet">Carter</a> (top) in 1979 and <a href="/wiki/Reagan_cabinet" class="mw-redirect" title="Reagan cabinet">Reagan</a> (bottom) in 1981</div></div></div><p>
Thatcher became Prime Minister on 4 May 1979. Arriving at Downing Street she said, paraphrasing the misattributed <a href="/wiki/Prayer_of_Saint_Francis" title="Prayer of Saint Francis">Prayer of Saint Francis</a>:</p><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><div class="poem">
<p>Where there is discord, may we bring harmony;<br />
Where there is error, may we bring truth;<br />
Where there is doubt, may we bring faith;<br />
And where there is despair, may we bring hope.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
</div>
</blockquote>
<p>Thatcher was to remain in office throughout the 1980s. For most of her premiership, she was described as the most powerful woman in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBern198743Ogden19909,_12_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBern198743Ogden19909,_12-111">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic_affairs">Domestic affairs</span></h3>
<p>Thatcher was Leader of the Opposition and Prime Minister at a time of increased racial tension in Britain. Commenting on the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_local_elections,_1977" title="United Kingdom local elections, 1977">local elections of 1977</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Economist" title="The Economist">The Economist</a></i> noted "The Tory tide swamped the smaller parties. That specifically includes the <a href="/wiki/National_Front_(UK)" title="National Front (UK)">National Front</a> (NF), which suffered a clear decline from last year".<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> Her standing in the polls rose by 11% after a January 1978 interview for <i><a href="/wiki/World_in_Action" title="World in Action">World in Action</a></i> in which she said "the British character has done so much for democracy, for law and done so much throughout the world that if there is any fear that it might be swamped people are going to react and be rather hostile to those coming in", as well as "in many ways &#91;minorities&#93; add to the richness and variety of this country. The moment the minority threatens to become a big one, people get frightened".<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> In the 1979 general election, the Conservatives attracted voters from the NF, whose support almost collapsed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReitan200326Ward2004128_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReitan200326Ward2004128-118">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> In a meeting in July 1979 with Foreign Secretary <a href="/wiki/Lord_Carrington" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Carrington">Lord Carrington</a> and Home Secretary <a href="/wiki/William_Whitelaw" class="mw-redirect" title="William Whitelaw">William Whitelaw</a> she objected to the number of Asian immigrants, in the context of limiting the total of <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_boat_people" title="Vietnamese boat people">Vietnamese boat people</a> allowed to settle in the UK to fewer than 10,000 over two years.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>
As Prime Minister, Thatcher met weekly with <a href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Queen Elizabeth II">Queen Elizabeth II</a> to discuss government business, and their relationship came under close scrutiny.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReitan200328Seward2001154_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReitan200328Seward2001154-120">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> Her biographer <a href="/wiki/John_Campbell_(biographer)" title="John Campbell (biographer)">John Campbell</a> writes:</p><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>One question that continued to fascinate the public about the phenomenon of a woman Prime Minister was how she got on with the Queen. The answer is that their relations were punctiliously correct, but there was little love lost on either side. As two women of very similar age&#160;&#8211;&#32;Mrs Thatcher was six months older&#160;&#8211;&#32;occupying parallel positions at the top of the social pyramid, one the head of government, the other head of state, they were bound to be in some sense rivals. Mrs Thatcher's attitude to the Queen was ambivalent. On the one hand she had an almost mystical reverence for the institution of the monarchy: she always made sure that Christmas dinner was finished in time for everyone to sit down solemnly to watch the Queen's broadcast. Yet at the same time she was trying to modernise the country and sweep away many of the values and practices which the monarchy perpetuated.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell2011464_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell2011464-121">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote> <p><a href="/wiki/Michael_Shea_(diplomat)" title="Michael Shea (diplomat)">Michael Shea</a>, the Queen's press secretary, had reportedly leaked anonymous rumours of a rift, which were officially denied by <a href="/wiki/William_Heseltine" title="William Heseltine">William Heseltine</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Private_Secretary_to_the_Sovereign" title="Private Secretary to the Sovereign">Private Secretary to the Sovereign</a>. Thatcher later wrote: "I always found the Queen's attitude towards the work of the Government absolutely correct&#160;... stories of clashes between 'two powerful women' were just too good not to make up."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThatcher199318_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThatcher199318-122">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>
</p><h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy_and_taxation">Economy and taxation</span></h4>
<table class="mw-datatable floatright" style="font-size:90%; margin-bottom:0.55em; margin-left:0.6em; text-align:center">

<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
</span></td></tr></tbody></table>
<table class="cquote pullquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">
<tbody><tr>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: &#39;Times New Roman&#39;, Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">“
</td>
<td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">You turn if you want to. <a href="/wiki/The_lady%27s_not_for_turning" title="The lady&#39;s not for turning">The lady's not for turning</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</td>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: &#39;Times New Roman&#39;, Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">”
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="3" class="cquotecite" style="border: none; padding-right: 4%; font-size: smaller; line-height:normal; text-align: right;"><cite>—&#8201;Thatcher to the Conservative Party conference in 1980</cite>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
