<p>Thatcher's economic policy was influenced by <a href="/wiki/Monetarism" title="Monetarism">monetarist</a> thinking and economists such as <a href="/wiki/Milton_Friedman" title="Milton Friedman">Milton Friedman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alan_Walters" title="Alan Walters">Alan Walters</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChilds2006185_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChilds2006185-130">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> Together with Chancellor of the Exchequer <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Howe" title="Geoffrey Howe">Geoffrey Howe</a>, she lowered direct taxes on income and increased indirect taxes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReitan200330_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReitan200330-131">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> She increased interest rates to slow the growth of the money supply and thereby lower inflation,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChilds2006185_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChilds2006185-130">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> introduced cash limits on public spending, and reduced expenditure on social services such as education and housing.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReitan200330_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReitan200330-131">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> Cuts to higher education resulted in her becoming the first Oxford-educated post-war Prime Minister without an honorary doctorate from <a href="/wiki/Oxford_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxford University">Oxford University</a>, after a 738–319 vote of the governing assembly and a student petition.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Her new centrally-funded <a href="/wiki/City_Technology_College" title="City Technology College">City Technology Colleges</a> did not achieve much success, and the Funding Agency for Schools was set up to control expenditure by opening and closing schools; a right-wing <a href="/wiki/Think_tank" title="Think tank">think tank</a> described it as having "an extraordinary range of dictatorial powers".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarr2007464_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarr2007464-133">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some Heathite Conservatives in the Cabinet, the so-called "<a href="/wiki/Wets_and_dries" title="Wets and dries">wets</a>", expressed doubt over Thatcher's policies.<sup id="cite_ref-nft_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nft-134">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/1981_England_riots" title="1981 England riots">1981 England riots</a> resulted in the British media discussing the need for a policy U-turn. At the 1980 Conservative Party conference, Thatcher addressed the issue directly, with a speech written by the playwright <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Millar" title="Ronald Millar">Ronald Millar</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones2007224_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones2007224-135">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> that included the lines: "You turn if you want to. The lady's not for turning."<sup id="cite_ref-nft_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nft-134">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thatcher's job approval rating fell to 23% by December 1980, lower than recorded for any previous Prime Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThornton200618_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThornton200618-136">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> As the <a href="/wiki/Early_1980s_recession" title="Early 1980s recession">recession of the early 1980s</a> deepened, she increased taxes,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReitan200331_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReitan200331-137">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> despite concerns expressed in a March 1981 statement signed by 364&#160;leading economists,<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> which argued there was "no basis in economic theory&#160;... for the Government's belief that by deflating demand they will bring inflation permanently under control", adding that "present policies will deepen the depression, erode the industrial base of our economy and threaten its social and political stability".<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By 1982, the UK began to experience signs of economic recovery;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFloudJohnson2004392_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFloudJohnson2004392-140">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> inflation was down to 8.6% from a high of 18%, but unemployment was over 3&#160;million for the first time since the 1930s.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> By 1983, overall economic growth was stronger, and inflation and mortgage rates had fallen to their lowest levels in 13 years, although manufacturing employment as a share of total employment fell to just over 30%,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERowthornWells1987234_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERowthornWells1987234-142">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> with total unemployment remaining high, peaking at 3.3&#160;million in 1984.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the 1982 Conservative Party Conference, Thatcher said: "We have done more to roll back the frontiers of socialism than any previous Conservative Government."<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> She claimed at the Party Conference the following year that the British people had completely rejected <a href="/wiki/State_socialism" title="State socialism">state socialism</a> and understood that "the state has no source of money other than money which people earn themselves&#160;... There is no such thing as public money; there is only taxpayers' money".<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By 1987, unemployment was falling, the economy was stable and strong and inflation was low. Opinion polls showed a comfortable Conservative lead, and <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_local_elections,_1987" title="United Kingdom local elections, 1987">local council election</a> results had also been successful, prompting Thatcher to call a general election for 11 June that year, despite the deadline for an election still being 12 months away. The <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1987" title="United Kingdom general election, 1987">election</a> saw Thatcher re-elected for a third successive term.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thatcher had been firmly opposed to British membership of the <a href="/wiki/European_Exchange_Rate_Mechanism" title="European Exchange Rate Mechanism">Exchange Rate Mechanism</a> (ERM, a precursor to <a href="/wiki/European_Monetary_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="European Monetary Union">European Monetary Union</a>), believing that it would constrain the British economy,<sup id="cite_ref-ecc_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ecc-147">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> despite the urging of both her Chancellor of the Exchequer <a href="/wiki/Nigel_Lawson" title="Nigel Lawson">Nigel Lawson</a> and Foreign Secretary <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Howe" title="Geoffrey Howe">Geoffrey Howe</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThatcher1993712_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThatcher1993712-148">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> in October 1990 she was persuaded by <a href="/wiki/John_Major" title="John Major">John Major</a>, Lawson's successor as Chancellor, to join the ERM at what proved to be too high a rate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarr2007484_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarr2007484-149">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thatcher reformed local government taxes by replacing <a href="/wiki/Rates_(tax)" title="Rates (tax)">domestic rates</a> (a tax based on the nominal rental value of a home) with the <a href="/wiki/Poll_tax_(Great_Britain)" title="Poll tax (Great Britain)">Community Charge</a> (or poll tax) in which the same amount was charged to each adult resident.<sup id="cite_ref-polltax_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-polltax-150">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> The new tax was introduced in Scotland in 1989 and in England and Wales the following year,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReitan200387–88_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReitan200387–88-151">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> and proved to be among the most unpopular policies of her premiership.<sup id="cite_ref-polltax_150-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-polltax-150">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> Public disquiet culminated in a 70,000 to 200,000-strong<sup id="cite_ref-trafalgarsq_num_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-trafalgarsq_num-152">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> demonstration in London in March 1990; the demonstration around <a href="/wiki/Trafalgar_Square" title="Trafalgar Square">Trafalgar Square</a> deteriorated into <a href="/wiki/Poll_tax_riots" title="Poll tax riots">riots</a>, leaving 113 people injured and 340 under arrest.<sup id="cite_ref-otd_pt_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-otd_pt-153">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> The Community Charge was abolished in 1991 by her successor, John Major.<sup id="cite_ref-otd_pt_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-otd_pt-153">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> It has since transpired that Thatcher herself had failed to register for the tax, and was threatened with financial penalties if she did not return her form.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_relations">Industrial relations</span></h4>
<table class="rquote pullquote floatright" role="presentation" style="border-collapse:collapse; border-style:none; float:right; margin:0.5em 0.75em; width:22em;">

<tbody><tr style="text-align:left; vertical-align:top;">
<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:&#39;Times New Roman&#39;,serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">“
</td>
<td style="padding:0 10px;">We had to fight the enemy without in the Falklands. We always have to be aware of the enemy within, which is much more difficult to fight and more dangerous to liberty.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKhabaz2007226_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKhabaz2007226-155">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>
</td>
<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:&#39;Times New Roman&#39;,serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; text-align:right; vertical-align:bottom; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">”
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td class="rquotecite" colspan="3" style="padding-top:10px; font-size:smaller; line-height:normal; text-align:right;"><cite>—&#160;&#32;Thatcher on the <a href="/wiki/UK_miners%27_strike_(1984%E2%80%9385)" title="UK miners&#39; strike (1984–85)">ongoing miners' dispute</a> in 1984</cite>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Thatcher believed that the <a href="/wiki/Trade_unions_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Trade unions in the United Kingdom">trade unions</a> were harmful to both ordinary trade unionists and the public.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell201189–90_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell201189–90-156">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> She was committed to reducing the power of the unions, whose leadership she accused of undermining parliamentary democracy and economic performance through strike action.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThatcher199397–98,_339–340_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThatcher199397–98,_339–340-157">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> Several unions launched strikes in response to legislation introduced to limit their power, but resistance eventually collapsed.<sup id="cite_ref-thatcher-cw_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thatcher-cw-158">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> Only 39% of union members voted Labour in the 1983 general election.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> According to the BBC in 2004, Thatcher "managed to destroy the power of the trade unions for almost a generation".<sup id="cite_ref-bbcstrike_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbcstrike-160">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/UK_miners%27_strike_(1984%E2%80%9385)" title="UK miners&#39; strike (1984–85)">miners' strike of 1984–85</a> was the biggest and most devastating confrontation between the unions and the government under Thatcher.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In March 1984, the <a href="/wiki/National_Coal_Board" title="National Coal Board">National Coal Board</a> (NCB) proposed to close 20 of the 174&#160;state-owned mines and cut 20,000&#160;jobs out of 187,000.<sup id="cite_ref-Glass_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glass-162">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Black_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Black-163">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pits-closed_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pits-closed-164">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> Two-thirds of the country's miners, led by the <a href="/wiki/National_Union_of_Mineworkers_(Great_Britain)" title="National Union of Mineworkers (Great Britain)">National Union of Mineworkers</a> (NUM) under <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Scargill" title="Arthur Scargill">Arthur Scargill</a>, downed tools in protest.<sup id="cite_ref-Glass_162-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glass-162">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-thatcher-num_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thatcher-num-165">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Jones_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones-166">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> However, Scargill refused to hold a ballot on the strike,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdeneyLloyd198888–89_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdeneyLloyd198888–89-167">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> having previously lost three ballots on a national strike (in January and October 1982, and March 1983).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdeneyLloyd1988169_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdeneyLloyd1988169-168">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> This led to the strike being declared illegal by the <a href="/wiki/High_Court_of_Justice" title="High Court of Justice">High Court of Justice</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdeneyLloyd1988170_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdeneyLloyd1988170-169">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thatcher refused to meet the union's demands and compared the miners' dispute to the <a href="/wiki/Falklands_War" title="Falklands War">Falklands War</a>, declaring in a speech in 1984: "We had to fight the enemy without in the Falklands. We always have to be aware of the enemy within, which is much more difficult to fight and more dangerous to liberty."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKhabaz2007226_155-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKhabaz2007226-155">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> Although Thatcher had only described the miners' leaders and left-wing authorities as the "enemy within", her opponents quickly misrepresented it as a reference to all miners and as a sign that she showed contempt for the organised working class. Consequently, the phrase was forever used against her.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore2015164_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore2015164-171">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After a year out on strike, in March 1985 the NUM leadership conceded without a deal. The cost to the economy was estimated to be at least £1.5&#160;billion, and the strike was blamed for much of the <a href="/wiki/Pound_sterling" title="Pound sterling">pound</a>'s fall against the <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">US dollar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Harper_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harper-172">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> Thatcher reflected on the end of the strike and said that "if anyone has won" it was "the miners who stayed at work" and all those "that have kept Britain going".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore2015178_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore2015178-173">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The government closed 25&#160;unprofitable coal mines in 1985, and by 1992 a total of 97 mines had been closed;<sup id="cite_ref-pits-closed_164-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pits-closed-164">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> those that remained were privatised in 1994.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> The resulting closure of 150&#160;coal mines, some of which were not losing money, resulted in the loss of tens of thousands of jobs and had the effect of devastating entire communities.<sup id="cite_ref-pits-closed_164-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pits-closed-164">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> Strikes had helped bring down Heath's government, and Thatcher was determined to succeed where he had failed. Her strategy of preparing fuel stocks, appointing hardliner <a href="/wiki/Ian_MacGregor" title="Ian MacGregor">Ian MacGregor</a> as NCB leader, and ensuring that police were adequately trained and equipped with riot gear, contributed to her triumph over the striking miners.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarr2007411_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarr2007411-175">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The number of stoppages across the UK peaked at 4,583 in 1979, when more than 29&#160;million working days had been lost. In 1984, the year of the miners' strike, there were 1,221, resulting in the loss of more than 27&#160;million working days. Stoppages then fell steadily throughout the rest of Thatcher's premiership; in 1990 there were 630 and fewer than 2&#160;million working days lost, and they continued to fall thereafter.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButlerButler1994375_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButlerButler1994375-176">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> Thatcher's tenure also witnessed a sharp decline in trade union density, with the percentage of workers belonging to a trade union falling from 57.3% in 1979 to 49.5% in 1985.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaybourn1992208_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaybourn1992208-177">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> In 1979 up until Thatcher's final year in office, trade union membership also fell, from 13.5&#160;million in 1979 to fewer than 10&#160;million.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarrell1994127_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarrell1994127-178">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Privatisation">Privatisation</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Margaret_Thatcher_visiting_Salford.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Photograph" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Margaret_Thatcher_visiting_Salford.jpg/220px-Margaret_Thatcher_visiting_Salford.jpg" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Margaret_Thatcher_visiting_Salford.jpg/330px-Margaret_Thatcher_visiting_Salford.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Margaret_Thatcher_visiting_Salford.jpg/440px-Margaret_Thatcher_visiting_Salford.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2131" data-file-height="1502" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Margaret_Thatcher_visiting_Salford.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thatcher during a visit to <a href="/wiki/Salford_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Salford University">Salford University</a> in 1982</div></div></div>
<p>The policy of <a href="/wiki/Privatisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Privatisation">privatisation</a> has been called "a crucial ingredient of Thatcherism".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeldonCollings200027_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeldonCollings200027-179">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> After the 1983 election the sale of state utilities accelerated;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeigenbaumHenigHamnett199871_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeigenbaumHenigHamnett199871-180">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> more than £29&#160;billion was raised from the sale of nationalised industries, and another £18&#160;billion from the sale of council houses.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarr2007428_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarr2007428-181">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> The process of privatisation, especially the preparation of nationalised industries for privatisation, was associated with marked improvements in performance, particularly in terms of <a href="/wiki/Workforce_productivity" title="Workforce productivity">labour productivity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEParkerMartin1995_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEParkerMartin1995-182">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some of the privatised industries, including gas, <a href="/wiki/Water_privatisation_in_England_and_Wales" title="Water privatisation in England and Wales">water</a>, and electricity, were <a href="/wiki/Natural_monopoly" title="Natural monopoly">natural monopolies</a> for which privatisation involved little increase in competition. The privatised industries that demonstrated improvement sometimes did so while still under state ownership. <a href="/wiki/British_Steel_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="British Steel Corporation">British Steel Corporation</a> had made great gains in profitability while still a nationalised industry under the government-appointed MacGregor chairmanship, which faced down trade-union opposition to close plants and halve the workforce.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> Regulation was also significantly expanded to compensate for the loss of direct government control, with the foundation of regulatory bodies such as <a href="/wiki/Oftel" title="Oftel">Oftel</a> (<a href="/wiki/Telecommunications_Act_1984" title="Telecommunications Act 1984">1984</a>), <a href="/wiki/Ofgas" class="mw-redirect" title="Ofgas">Ofgas</a> (<a href="/wiki/Gas_Act_1986" class="mw-redirect" title="Gas Act 1986">1986</a>), and the <a href="/wiki/National_Rivers_Authority" title="National Rivers Authority">National Rivers Authority</a> (<a href="/wiki/Water_Act_1989" title="Water Act 1989">1989</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVeljanovski1990291–304_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVeljanovski1990291–304-184">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> There was no clear pattern to the degree of competition, regulation, and performance among the privatised industries.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEParkerMartin1995_182-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEParkerMartin1995-182">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In most cases privatisation benefited consumers in terms of lower prices and improved efficiency, but results overall have been mixed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcAleese2004169–70_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcAleese2004169–70-185">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> Not all privatised companies have had successful share price trajectories in the longer term.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> A 2010 review by the <a href="/wiki/Institute_of_Economic_Affairs" title="Institute of Economic Affairs">Institute of Economic Affairs</a> states that "it does seem to be the case that once competition and/or effective regulation was introduced, performance improved markedly&#160;... But I hasten to emphasise again that the literature is not unanimous".<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thatcher always resisted <a href="/wiki/Privatisation_of_British_Rail" title="Privatisation of British Rail">privatising British Rail</a> and was said to have told Transport Secretary <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Ridley,_Baron_Ridley_of_Liddesdale" title="Nicholas Ridley, Baron Ridley of Liddesdale">Nicholas Ridley</a> that "Railway privatisation will be the <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Waterloo#Noun" class="extiw" title="wikt:Waterloo">Waterloo</a> of this government. Please never mention the railways to me again". Shortly before her resignation in 1990, she accepted the arguments for privatisation, which her successor <a href="/wiki/John_Major" title="John Major">John Major</a> implemented in 1994.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarr2007495_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarr2007495-188">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The privatisation of public assets was combined with <a href="/wiki/Deregulation#United_Kingdom" title="Deregulation">financial deregulation</a> in an attempt to fuel economic growth. Chancellor <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Howe" title="Geoffrey Howe">Geoffrey Howe</a> abolished the UK's exchange controls in 1979,<sup id="cite_ref-Robertson_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Robertson-189">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> which allowed more capital to be invested in foreign markets, and the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang_(financial_markets)" title="Big Bang (financial markets)">Big Bang</a> of 1986 removed many restrictions on the <a href="/wiki/London_Stock_Exchange" title="London Stock Exchange">London Stock Exchange</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Robertson_189-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Robertson-189">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Northern_Ireland">Northern Ireland</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Margaret_Thatcher_on_a_visit_to_Northern_Ireland.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Photograph" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Margaret_Thatcher_on_a_visit_to_Northern_Ireland.jpg/220px-Margaret_Thatcher_on_a_visit_to_Northern_Ireland.jpg" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Margaret_Thatcher_on_a_visit_to_Northern_Ireland.jpg/330px-Margaret_Thatcher_on_a_visit_to_Northern_Ireland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Margaret_Thatcher_on_a_visit_to_Northern_Ireland.jpg/440px-Margaret_Thatcher_on_a_visit_to_Northern_Ireland.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2596" data-file-height="1924" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Margaret_Thatcher_on_a_visit_to_Northern_Ireland.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Margaret and <a href="/wiki/Denis_Thatcher" title="Denis Thatcher">Denis Thatcher</a> on a visit to <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a> in late 1982</div></div></div>
<p>In 1980 and 1981, <a href="/wiki/Provisional_Irish_Republican_Army" title="Provisional Irish Republican Army">Provisional Irish Republican Army</a> (PIRA) and <a href="/wiki/Irish_National_Liberation_Army" title="Irish National Liberation Army">Irish National Liberation Army</a> (INLA) prisoners in Northern Ireland's <a href="/wiki/HM_Prison_Maze" title="HM Prison Maze">Maze Prison</a> carried out <a href="/wiki/1981_Irish_hunger_strike" title="1981 Irish hunger strike">hunger strikes</a> in an effort to regain the status of political prisoners that had been removed in 1976 by the preceding Labour government.<sup id="cite_ref-strike_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-strike-190">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bobby_Sands" title="Bobby Sands">Bobby Sands</a> began the 1981 strike, saying that he would fast until death unless prison inmates won concessions over their living conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-strike_190-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-strike-190">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> Thatcher refused to countenance a return to political status for the prisoners, having declared "Crime is crime is crime; it is not political",<sup id="cite_ref-strike_190-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-strike-190">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, the British government privately contacted republican leaders in a bid to bring the hunger strikes to an end.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> After the deaths of Sands and nine others, the strike ended. Some rights were restored to paramilitary prisoners, but not official recognition of political status.<sup id="cite_ref-CAIN-hs_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CAIN-hs-192">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> Violence in Northern Ireland escalated significantly during the hunger strikes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEnglish2005207–08_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEnglish2005207–08-193">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thatcher narrowly escaped injury in an IRA <a href="/wiki/Brighton_hotel_bombing" title="Brighton hotel bombing">assassination attempt</a> at a Brighton hotel early in the morning on 12 October 1984.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc-bomb_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-bomb-194">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> Five people were killed, including the wife of minister <a href="/wiki/John_Wakeham" title="John Wakeham">John Wakeham</a>. Thatcher was staying at the hotel to prepare for the Conservative Party conference, which she insisted should open as scheduled the following day.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc-bomb_194-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-bomb-194">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> She delivered her speech as planned,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThatcher1993379–383_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThatcher1993379–383-195">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> though rewritten from her original draft,<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> in a move that was widely supported across the political spectrum and enhanced her popularity with the public.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELanoueHeadrick1998_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELanoueHeadrick1998-197">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 6 November 1981, Thatcher and Irish Taoiseach <a href="/wiki/Garret_FitzGerald" title="Garret FitzGerald">Garret FitzGerald</a> had established the Anglo-Irish Inter-Governmental Council, a forum for meetings between the two governments.<sup id="cite_ref-CAIN-hs_192-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CAIN-hs-192">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> On 15 November 1985, Thatcher and FitzGerald signed the Hillsborough <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Irish_Agreement" title="Anglo-Irish Agreement">Anglo-Irish Agreement</a>, which marked the first time a British government had given the Republic of Ireland an advisory role in the governance of Northern Ireland. In protest, the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Says_No" title="Ulster Says No">Ulster Says No</a> movement led by <a href="/wiki/Ian_Paisley" title="Ian Paisley">Ian Paisley</a> attracted 100,000 to a rally in Belfast,<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ian_Gow" title="Ian Gow">Ian Gow</a>, later assassinated by the PIRA, resigned as <a href="/wiki/Minister_of_State" title="Minister of State">Minister of State</a> in the <a href="/wiki/HM_Treasury" title="HM Treasury">HM Treasury</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoloney2002336_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoloney2002336-200">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> and all 15 Unionist MPs resigned their parliamentary seats; only one was not returned in the subsequent <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_Kingdom_by-elections_(1979%E2%80%932010)" title="List of United Kingdom by-elections (1979–2010)">by-elections</a> on 23 January 1986.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECochrane2001143_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECochrane2001143-201">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environment">Environment</span></h3>
<p>Thatcher supported an active <a href="/wiki/Climate_protection" class="mw-redirect" title="Climate protection">climate protection</a> policy and was instrumental in the passing of the <a href="/wiki/Environmental_Protection_Act_1990" title="Environmental Protection Act 1990">Environmental Protection Act 1990</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETewdwr-Jones200347_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETewdwr-Jones200347-202">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change</a>, and in founding the <a href="/wiki/Hadley_Centre_for_Climate_Prediction_and_Research" title="Hadley Centre for Climate Prediction and Research">Hadley Centre for Climate Research and Prediction</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Harrabin_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harrabin-203">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> She helped to put <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acid_rain" title="Acid rain">acid rain</a> and general pollution in the British mainstream in the late 1980s,<sup id="cite_ref-Harrabin_203-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harrabin-203">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell2011642_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell2011642-205">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> and in 1989 she called for a global treaty on climate change.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> Her speeches included one to the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a> on 27 September 1988<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> and to the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly" title="United Nations General Assembly">UN General Assembly</a> in November 1989. However, following her retirement as Prime Minister in 1990, she became sceptical about <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_global_warming" title="Politics of global warming">climate change policy</a> and rejected <a href="/wiki/Climate_alarmism" class="mw-redirect" title="Climate alarmism">climate alarmism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_affairs">Foreign affairs</span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Thatcher_at_Oval_Office_desk_with_Carter.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Thatcher_at_Oval_Office_desk_with_Carter.jpg/200px-Thatcher_at_Oval_Office_desk_with_Carter.jpg" width="200" height="141" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Thatcher_at_Oval_Office_desk_with_Carter.jpg/300px-Thatcher_at_Oval_Office_desk_with_Carter.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Thatcher_at_Oval_Office_desk_with_Carter.jpg/400px-Thatcher_at_Oval_Office_desk_with_Carter.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1002" data-file-height="705" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Thatcher with President <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Oval_Office" title="Oval Office">Oval Office</a>, 1979</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Reagan_et_Thatcher.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Reagan_et_Thatcher.jpg/200px-Reagan_et_Thatcher.jpg" width="200" height="133" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Reagan_et_Thatcher.jpg/300px-Reagan_et_Thatcher.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Reagan_et_Thatcher.jpg/400px-Reagan_et_Thatcher.jpg 2x" data-file-width="827" data-file-height="551" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Thatcher with President <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> in the Oval Office, 1988</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Thatcher_and_Bush_-_1990_-_P14935-18A.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Thatcher_and_Bush_-_1990_-_P14935-18A.jpg/200px-Thatcher_and_Bush_-_1990_-_P14935-18A.jpg" width="200" height="134" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Thatcher_and_Bush_-_1990_-_P14935-18A.jpg/300px-Thatcher_and_Bush_-_1990_-_P14935-18A.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Thatcher_and_Bush_-_1990_-_P14935-18A.jpg/400px-Thatcher_and_Bush_-_1990_-_P14935-18A.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1239" data-file-height="831" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Thatcher with President <a href="/wiki/George_H._W._Bush" title="George H. W. Bush">George H. W. Bush</a> in <a href="/wiki/Aspen,_Colorado" title="Aspen, Colorado">Aspen, Colorado</a>, 1990</div></div></div></div>
<p>Thatcher appointed <a href="/wiki/Lord_Carrington" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Carrington">Lord Carrington</a>, a senior member of the party and former <a href="/wiki/Minister_of_Defence_(United_Kingdom)" title="Minister of Defence (United Kingdom)">Minister of Defence</a>, as Foreign Minister in 1979.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESkedCook1993364–422_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESkedCook1993364–422-209">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> Although he was considered a "wet", he avoided domestic affairs and got along well with Thatcher. The first issue was what to do with <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia" title="Rhodesia">Rhodesia</a>, where the five-percent white population was determined to rule the prosperous, largely-black ex-colony in the face of overwhelming international disapproval. After the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese Empire</a> in Africa in 1975, South Africa, which had been Rhodesia's chief supporter, realised that country was a liability. Black rule was inevitable, and Carrington brokered a peaceful solution at the <a href="/wiki/Lancaster_House_Agreement" title="Lancaster House Agreement">Lancaster House conference</a> in December 1979, attended by Rhodesian Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Ian_Smith" title="Ian Smith">Ian Smith</a>, as well as the key black leaders: <a href="/wiki/Abel_Muzorewa" title="Abel Muzorewa">Abel Muzorewa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Mugabe" title="Robert Mugabe">Robert Mugabe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joshua_Nkomo" title="Joshua Nkomo">Joshua Nkomo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Josiah_Tongogara" title="Josiah Tongogara">Josiah Tongogara</a>. The conference ended the <a href="/wiki/Rhodesian_Bush_War" title="Rhodesian Bush War">Rhodesian Bush War</a>. The end result was the new nation of Zimbabwe under black rule in 1980.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewis1980Soames1980_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewis1980Soames1980-210">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cold_War">Cold War</span></h4>
<p>Thatcher's first foreign policy crisis came with the 1979 <a href="/wiki/Soviet_invasion_of_Afghanistan" class="mw-redirect" title="Soviet invasion of Afghanistan">Soviet invasion of Afghanistan</a>. She condemned the invasion, said it showed the bankruptcy of a <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A9tente" title="Détente">détente</a> policy, and helped convince some British athletes to boycott the <a href="/wiki/1980_Moscow_Olympics" class="mw-redirect" title="1980 Moscow Olympics">1980 Moscow Olympics</a>. She gave weak support to US President <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a> who tried to punish the USSR with economic sanctions. Britain's economic situation was precarious, and most of NATO was reluctant to cut trade ties.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELahey2013_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELahey2013-211">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Financial_Times" title="Financial Times">Financial Times</a></i> reported that her government had secretly supplied <a href="/wiki/Saddam_Hussein" title="Saddam Hussein">Saddam Hussein</a> with <a href="/wiki/British_support_for_Iraq_during_the_Iran%E2%80%93Iraq_war" title="British support for Iraq during the Iran–Iraq war">military equipment since 1981</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Thatcher_Hussein_secret_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thatcher_Hussein_secret-212">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thatcher and her government backed the <a href="/wiki/Khmer_Rouge" title="Khmer Rouge">Khmer Rouge</a> keeping their seat in the UN after they were ousted from power in Cambodia by the <a href="/wiki/Cambodian%E2%80%93Vietnamese_War" title="Cambodian–Vietnamese War">Cambodian–Vietnamese War</a>. Although Thatcher denied it at the time, it was revealed in 1991 that from 1983 the <a href="/wiki/Special_Air_Service" title="Special Air Service">SAS</a> was sent to secretly train the "non-Communist" members of the <a href="/wiki/Coalition_Government_of_Democratic_Kampuchea" title="Coalition Government of Democratic Kampuchea">CGDK</a> to fight against the Vietnamese-backed <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_Kampuchea" title="People&#39;s Republic of Kampuchea">Kampuchea</a> (PRK) government.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-newst_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newst-216">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> The "non-communist members", such as the <a href="/wiki/FUNCINPEC" title="FUNCINPEC">Sihanoukists</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Khmer_People%27s_National_Liberation_Front" title="Khmer People&#39;s National Liberation Front">Khmer People's National Liberation Front</a>, "were dominated, diplomatically and militarily, by the Khmer Rouge". It was reported that the SAS had taught "the use of improvised explosive devices, booby traps and the manufacture and use of time-delay devices", in what activist <a href="/wiki/Rae_McGrath" title="Rae McGrath">Rae McGrath</a> denounced as "a criminally irresponsible and cynical policy".<sup id="cite_ref-newst_216-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newst-216">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on an unreliable source. (September 2018)">unreliable source?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Thatcher was one of the first Western leaders to respond warmly to reformist Soviet leader <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Gorbachev" title="Mikhail Gorbachev">Mikhail Gorbachev</a>. Following Reagan–Gorbachev summit meetings and reforms enacted by Gorbachev in the USSR, she declared in November 1988 that "We're not in a Cold War now", but rather in a "new relationship much wider than the Cold War ever was".<sup id="cite_ref-reforms1988_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reforms1988-217">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> She went on a state visit to the Soviet Union in 1984 and met with Gorbachev and Council of Ministers Chairman <a href="/wiki/Nikolai_Ryzhkov" title="Nikolai Ryzhkov">Nikolai Ryzhkov</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZemcovFarrar2007138_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZemcovFarrar2007138-218">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ties_with_the_US">Ties with the US</span></h4>
<p>Thatcher became closely aligned with the <a href="/wiki/Reagan_Doctrine" title="Reagan Doctrine">Cold War policies</a> of US President <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a>, based on their shared <a href="/wiki/Anti-communism" title="Anti-communism">distrust of Communism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-thatcher-cw_158-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thatcher-cw-158">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> A disagreement came in 1983 when Reagan did not consult with her on the <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Grenada" class="mw-redirect" title="Invasion of Grenada">invasion of Grenada</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams2001_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams2001-219">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> During her first year as Prime Minister she supported <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a>'s decision to deploy US nuclear <a href="/wiki/BGM-109G_Ground_Launched_Cruise_Missile" title="BGM-109G Ground Launched Cruise Missile">cruise</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pershing_II" title="Pershing II">Pershing II</a> missiles in Western Europe,<sup id="cite_ref-thatcher-cw_158-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thatcher-cw-158">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> permitting the US to station more than 160&#160;cruise missiles at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Greenham_Common" title="RAF Greenham Common">RAF Greenham Common</a>, starting on 14 November 1983 and triggering mass protests by the <a href="/wiki/Campaign_for_Nuclear_Disarmament" title="Campaign for Nuclear Disarmament">Campaign for Nuclear Disarmament</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-thatcher-cw_158-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thatcher-cw-158">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> She bought the <a href="/wiki/Trident_nuclear_programme" class="mw-redirect" title="Trident nuclear programme">Trident</a> nuclear missile submarine system from the US to replace Polaris, tripling the UK's nuclear forces<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> at an eventual cost of more than £12&#160;billion (at 1996–97 prices).<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> Thatcher's preference for defence ties with the US was demonstrated in the <a href="/wiki/Westland_affair" title="Westland affair">Westland affair</a> of 1985–86, when she acted with colleagues to allow the struggling helicopter manufacturer <a href="/wiki/Westland_Helicopters" title="Westland Helicopters">Westland</a> to refuse a takeover offer from the Italian firm <a href="/wiki/Agusta" title="Agusta">Agusta</a> in favour of the management's preferred option, a link with <a href="/wiki/Sikorsky_Aircraft" title="Sikorsky Aircraft">Sikorsky Aircraft</a>. Defence Secretary <a href="/wiki/Michael_Heseltine" title="Michael Heseltine">Michael Heseltine</a>, who had supported the Agusta deal, resigned from the government in protest.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarr2007419_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarr2007419-223">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In April 1986 she permitted US <a href="/wiki/General_Dynamics_F-111_Aardvark" title="General Dynamics F-111 Aardvark">F-111s</a> to use <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">Royal Air Force</a> bases for the <a href="/wiki/1986_United_States_bombing_of_Libya" title="1986 United States bombing of Libya">bombing of Libya</a> in retaliation for the alleged <a href="/wiki/1986_West_Berlin_discotheque_bombing" title="1986 West Berlin discotheque bombing">Libyan bombing of a Berlin discothèque</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Cannon_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cannon-224">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> citing the right of self-defence under <a href="/wiki/Chapter_VII_of_the_United_Nations_Charter" title="Chapter VII of the United Nations Charter">Article 51 of the UN Charter</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;nb 5&#93;</a></sup> Polls suggested that fewer than one in three British citizens approved of her decision.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thatcher was in the US on a state visit when Iraqi leader <a href="/wiki/Saddam_Hussein" title="Saddam Hussein">Saddam Hussein</a> invaded neighbouring <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a> in <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Kuwait" title="Invasion of Kuwait">August 1990</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-gw-pbs_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gw-pbs-229">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> During her talks with President <a href="/wiki/George_H._W._Bush" title="George H. W. Bush">George H. W. Bush</a>, who succeeded Reagan in 1989, she recommended intervention,<sup id="cite_ref-gw-pbs_229-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gw-pbs-229">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> and put pressure on Bush to deploy troops in the Middle East to drive the <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Army" title="Iraqi Army">Iraqi Army</a> out of Kuwait.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup> Bush was apprehensive about the plan, prompting Thatcher to remark to him during a telephone conversation that "This was no time to go wobbly!"<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> Thatcher's government supplied military forces to the international coalition in the build-up to the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">Gulf War</a>, but she had resigned by the time hostilities began on 17 January 1991.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAitken2013600–601_233-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAitken2013600–601-233">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-grice_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grice-234">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> She applauded the coalition victory as a backbencher, but warned that "the victories of peace will take longer than the battles of war".<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> It was later disclosed that Thatcher suggested threatening Saddam with <a href="/wiki/Chemical_weapon" title="Chemical weapon">chemical weapons</a> after the invasion of Kuwait.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Crisis_in_the_Falklands">Crisis in the Falklands</span></h4>
<p>On 2 April 1982 the ruling <a href="/wiki/National_Reorganization_Process" title="National Reorganization Process">military junta in Argentina</a> ordered the invasion of the British possessions of the <a href="/wiki/Falkland_Islands" title="Falkland Islands">Falkland Islands</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Georgia_and_the_South_Sandwich_Islands" title="South Georgia and the South Sandwich Islands">South Georgia</a>, triggering the <a href="/wiki/Falklands_War" title="Falklands War">Falklands War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith198921_238-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith198921-238">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Occupation_of_the_Falkland_Islands" title="Occupation of the Falkland Islands">subsequent crisis</a> was "a defining moment of her &#91;Thatcher's&#93; premiership".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJackling2005230_239-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJackling2005230-239">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup> At the suggestion of <a href="/wiki/Harold_Macmillan" title="Harold Macmillan">Harold Macmillan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Armstrong,_Baron_Armstrong_of_Ilminster" title="Robert Armstrong, Baron Armstrong of Ilminster">Robert Armstrong</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJackling2005230_239-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJackling2005230-239">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup> she set up and chaired a small <a href="/wiki/War_cabinet#Falklands_War" title="War cabinet">War Cabinet</a> (formally called ODSA, Overseas and Defence committee, South Atlantic) to oversee the conduct of the war,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHastingsJenkins198380–81_240-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHastingsJenkins198380–81-240">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup> which by 5–6 April had authorised and dispatched a <a href="/wiki/British_naval_forces_in_the_Falklands_War" title="British naval forces in the Falklands War">naval task force</a> to retake the islands.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHastingsJenkins198395_241-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHastingsJenkins198395-241">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup> Argentina <a href="/wiki/Argentine_surrender_in_the_Falklands_War" title="Argentine surrender in the Falklands War">surrendered on 14 June</a> and <i>Operation Corporate</i> was hailed a success, notwithstanding the deaths of 255&#160;British servicemen and 3&#160;Falkland Islanders. Argentine fatalities totalled 649, half of them after the nuclear-powered submarine <a href="/wiki/HMS_Conqueror_(S48)" title="HMS Conqueror (S48)">HMS&#160;<i>Conqueror</i></a> torpedoed and sank the cruiser <a href="/wiki/ARA_General_Belgrano" title="ARA General Belgrano">ARA&#160;<i>General Belgrano</i></a> on 2 May.<sup id="cite_ref-liberation_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-liberation-242">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thatcher was criticised for the neglect of the Falklands' defence that <a href="/wiki/Events_leading_to_the_Falklands_War" title="Events leading to the Falklands War">led to the war</a>, and especially by <a href="/wiki/Tam_Dalyell" title="Tam Dalyell">Tam Dalyell</a> in Parliament for the decision to torpedo the <i>General Belgrano</i>, but overall she was considered a highly capable and committed war leader.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHastingsJenkins1983335–336_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHastingsJenkins1983335–336-243">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> The "<a href="/wiki/Falklands_factor" class="mw-redirect" title="Falklands factor">Falklands factor</a>", an economic recovery beginning early in 1982, and a bitterly divided opposition all contributed to Thatcher's second election victory in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1983" title="United Kingdom general election, 1983">1983</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESandersWardMarsh1987_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESandersWardMarsh1987-244">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup> Thatcher frequently referred after the war to the "Falklands spirit";<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> journalists <a href="/wiki/Max_Hastings" title="Max Hastings">Max Hastings</a> and <a href="/wiki/Simon_Jenkins" title="Simon Jenkins">Simon Jenkins</a> suggested in 1983 that this reflected her preference for the streamlined decision-making of her War Cabinet over the painstaking deal-making of peacetime <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Cabinet of the United Kingdom">cabinet government</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHastingsJenkins1983329_246-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHastingsJenkins1983329-246">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Negotiating_Hong_Kong">Negotiating Hong Kong</span></h4>
<p>In September 1982 she visited China to discuss with <a href="/wiki/Deng_Xiaoping" title="Deng Xiaoping">Deng Xiaoping</a> the <a href="/wiki/Transfer_of_sovereignty_over_Hong_Kong" class="mw-redirect" title="Transfer of sovereignty over Hong Kong">sovereignty of Hong Kong</a> after 1997. China was the first communist state Thatcher had visited and she was the first British prime minister to visit China. Throughout their meeting, she sought the PRC's agreement to a continued British presence in the territory. Deng insisted that the PRC's sovereignty on Hong Kong was non-negotiable, but stated his willingness to settle the sovereignty issue with the British government through formal negotiations, and both governments promised to maintain Hong Kong's stability and prosperity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYahuda1996155_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYahuda1996155-247">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup> After the two-year negotiations, Thatcher conceded to the PRC government and signed the <a href="/wiki/Sino-British_Joint_Declaration" title="Sino-British Joint Declaration">Sino-British Joint Declaration</a> in Beijing in 1984, agreeing to hand over Hong Kong's sovereignty in 1997.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReitan2003116_248-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReitan2003116-248">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Apartheid_in_South_Africa">Apartheid in South Africa</span></h4>
<p>Despite saying that she was in favour of "peaceful negotiations" to end <a href="/wiki/Apartheid" title="Apartheid">apartheid</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup> Thatcher opposed <a href="/wiki/Disinvestment_from_South_Africa" title="Disinvestment from South Africa">sanctions imposed on South Africa</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth</a> and the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">European Economic Community</a> (EEC).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell2011322_251-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell2011322-251">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup> She attempted to preserve trade with South Africa while persuading the government there to abandon apartheid. This included "[c]asting herself as President <a href="/wiki/P._W._Botha" title="P. W. Botha">Botha</a>'s candid friend", and inviting him to visit the UK in 1984,<sup id="cite_ref-Hanning_252-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hanning-252">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup> in spite of the "inevitable demonstrations" against his government.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell2011325_253-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell2011325-253">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup> Alan Merrydew of the Canadian broadcaster <a href="/wiki/CHAN-DT" title="CHAN-DT">BCTV News</a> asked Thatcher what her response was "to a reported ANC statement that they will target British firms in South Africa?", to which she later replied, "... when the ANC says that they will target British companies&#160;... This shows what a typical terrorist organisation it is. I fought terrorism all my life and if more people fought it, and we were all more successful, we should not have it and I hope that everyone in this hall will think it is right to go on fighting terrorism."<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup> During his visit to Britain five months after his release from prison, <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Mandela" title="Nelson Mandela">Nelson Mandela</a> praised Thatcher: "She is an enemy of apartheid&#160;... We have much to thank her for."<sup id="cite_ref-Hanning_252-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hanning-252">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span></h4>
<table class="rquote pullquote floatleft" role="presentation" style="border-collapse:collapse; border-style:none; float:left; margin:0.5em 0.75em; width:22em;">

<tbody><tr style="text-align:left; vertical-align:top;">
<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:&#39;Times New Roman&#39;,serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">“
</td>
<td style="padding:0 10px;">We have not successfully rolled back the frontiers of the state in Britain, only to see them re-imposed at a European level, with a European super-state exercising a new dominance from Brussels.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESenden20049_255-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESenden20049-255">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup>
</td>
<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:&#39;Times New Roman&#39;,serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; text-align:right; vertical-align:bottom; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">”
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td class="rquotecite" colspan="3" style="padding-top:10px; font-size:smaller; line-height:normal; text-align:right;"><cite>—&#160;&#32;Thatcher to the <a href="/wiki/College_of_Europe" title="College of Europe">College of Europe</a> in 1988</cite>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Thatcher and her party supported British membership of the EEC in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_European_Communities_membership_referendum,_1975" title="United Kingdom European Communities membership referendum, 1975">1975 national referendum</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-upi19750604_256-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-upi19750604-256">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup> but she believed that the role of the organisation should be limited to ensuring free trade and effective competition, and feared that the EEC's approach was at odds with her views on smaller government and deregulation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESenden20049_255-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESenden20049-255">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup> Her opposition to further <a href="/wiki/European_integration" title="European integration">European integration</a> became more pronounced during her premiership and particularly after her third election victory in 1987. During a 1988 speech in <a href="/wiki/Bruges" title="Bruges">Bruges</a> she outlined her opposition to proposals from the EEC, forerunner of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, for a federal structure and increased centralisation of decision making.<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup> She said: "We have not successfully rolled back the frontiers of the state in Britain, only to see them re-imposed at a European level, with a European super-state exercising a new dominance from Brussels."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESenden20049_255-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESenden20049-255">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thatcher, sharing the concerns of French President <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Mitterrand" title="François Mitterrand">François Mitterrand</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup> was initially opposed to <a href="/wiki/German_reunification" title="German reunification">German reunification</a>, telling Gorbachev that it "would lead to a change to postwar borders, and we cannot allow that because such a development would undermine the stability of the whole international situation and could endanger our security". She expressed concern that a united Germany would align itself more closely with the Soviet Union and move away from NATO.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGörtemaker2006198_259-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGörtemaker2006198-259">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> In March 1990, West German Chancellor <a href="/wiki/Helmut_Kohl" title="Helmut Kohl">Helmut Kohl</a> reassured Thatcher that he would keep her "informed of all his intentions about unification",<sup id="cite_ref-Bowcott_260-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bowcott-260">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup> and that he was prepared to disclose "matters which even his cabinet would not know".<sup id="cite_ref-Bowcott_260-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bowcott-260">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup> In November 1989, Thatcher hailed the fall of the <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Wall" title="Berlin Wall">Berlin Wall</a> as "a great day for freedom".<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Challenges_to_leadership_and_resignation">Challenges to leadership and resignation</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)_leadership_election,_1989" title="Conservative Party (UK) leadership election, 1989">Conservative Party leadership election, 1989</a> and <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)_leadership_election,_1990" title="Conservative Party (UK) leadership election, 1990">Conservative Party leadership election, 1990</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thatcher_reviews_troops.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Photograph" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Thatcher_reviews_troops.jpg/170px-Thatcher_reviews_troops.jpg" width="170" height="190" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Thatcher_reviews_troops.jpg/255px-Thatcher_reviews_troops.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Thatcher_reviews_troops.jpg/340px-Thatcher_reviews_troops.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1738" data-file-height="1942" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thatcher_reviews_troops.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thatcher reviewing the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Bermuda_Regiment" title="Royal Bermuda Regiment">Royal Bermuda Regiment</a> in early 1990</div></div></div>
<p>Thatcher was challenged for the leadership of the Conservative Party by the little-known backbench MP <a href="/wiki/Sir_Anthony_Meyer,_3rd_Baronet" title="Sir Anthony Meyer, 3rd Baronet">Sir Anthony Meyer</a> in the 1989 leadership election.<sup id="cite_ref-89election_262-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89election-262">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup> Of the 374 Conservative MPs eligible to vote, 314 voted for Thatcher and 33 for Meyer. Her supporters in the party viewed the result as a success, and rejected suggestions that there was discontent within the party.<sup id="cite_ref-89election_262-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89election-262">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During her premiership Thatcher had the second-lowest average approval rating (40%) of any post-war Prime Minister. Since the resignation of <a href="/wiki/Nigel_Lawson" title="Nigel Lawson">Nigel Lawson</a> as Chancellor in October 1989,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrewe1991_263-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrewe1991-263">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup> polls consistently showed that she was less popular than her party.<sup id="cite_ref-ridley_264-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ridley-264">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup> A self-described conviction politician, Thatcher always insisted that she did not care about her poll ratings and pointed instead to her unbeaten election record.<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Opinion polls in September 1990 reported that Labour had established a 14% lead over the Conservatives,<sup id="cite_ref-howe_266-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-howe-266">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup> and by November the Conservatives had been trailing Labour for 18&#160;months.<sup id="cite_ref-ridley_264-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ridley-264">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup> These ratings, together with Thatcher's combative personality and tendency to override collegiate opinion, contributed to discontent within her party.<sup id="cite_ref-resign-nyt_267-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-resign-nyt-267">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>
Thatcher removed <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Howe" title="Geoffrey Howe">Geoffrey Howe</a> as <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Foreign_and_Commonwealth_Affairs" title="Secretary of State for Foreign and Commonwealth Affairs">Foreign Secretary</a> in July 1989 after he and Lawson had forced her to agree to a plan for Britain to join the <a href="/wiki/European_Exchange_Rate_Mechanism" title="European Exchange Rate Mechanism">European Exchange Rate Mechanism</a> (ERM). Britain joined the ERM in October 1990. On 1 November 1990, Howe, by then the last remaining member of Thatcher's original 1979 cabinet, resigned from his position as <a href="/wiki/Deputy_Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Deputy Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Deputy Prime Minister</a>, ostensibly over her open hostility to moves towards <a href="/wiki/European_Monetary_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="European Monetary Union">European Monetary Union</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-howe_266-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-howe-266">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup> In his resignation speech on 13 November, Howe commented on Thatcher's openly dismissive attitude to the government's proposal for a new European currency competing against existing currencies (a "hard <a href="/wiki/European_Currency_Unit" title="European Currency Unit">ECU</a>"):</p><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>How on earth are the Chancellor and the Governor of the Bank of England, commending the hard ecu as they strive to, to be taken as serious participants in the debate against that kind of background noise? I believe that both the Chancellor and the Governor are cricketing enthusiasts, so I hope that there is no monopoly of cricketing metaphors. It is rather like sending your opening batsmen to the crease only for them to find, the moment the first balls are bowled, that their bats have been broken before the game by the team captain.<sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote><p> Howe's resignation hastened the end to Thatcher's premiership.<sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 14 November, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Heseltine" title="Michael Heseltine">Michael Heseltine</a> mounted a challenge for the leadership of the Conservative Party.<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarr2007473_273-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarr2007473-273">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup> Opinion polls had indicated that he would give the Conservatives a national lead over Labour.<sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup> Although Thatcher led on the first ballot with the votes of 204 Conservative MPs (54.8%) to 152 votes (40.9%) for Heseltine and 16 abstentions, she was four votes short of the required 15% majority. A second ballot was therefore necessary.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams199866_275-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams199866-275">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup> Thatcher initially declared her intention to "fight on and fight to win" the second ballot, but consultation with her Cabinet persuaded her to withdraw.<sup id="cite_ref-resign-nyt_267-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-resign-nyt-267">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-resign-bbc_276-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-resign-bbc-276">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup> After holding an audience with the Queen, calling other world leaders, and making one final Commons speech,<sup id="cite_ref-277" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-277">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup> on 28 November she left Downing Street in tears. She reportedly regarded her ousting as a betrayal.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarr2007474_278-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarr2007474-278">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup> Her resignation was a shock to many outside Britain, with foreign observers such as <a href="/wiki/Henry_Kissinger" title="Henry Kissinger">Henry Kissinger</a> and Gorbachev privately expressing consternation.<sup id="cite_ref-279" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-279">&#91;274&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thatcher was replaced as Prime Minister and party leader by Chancellor <a href="/wiki/John_Major" title="John Major">John Major</a>, who prevailed over Heseltine in the subsequent ballot. Major oversaw an upturn in Conservative support in the 17&#160;months leading to the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1992" title="United Kingdom general election, 1992">1992 general election</a> and led the party to a fourth successive victory on 9 April 1992.<sup id="cite_ref-280" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-280">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup> Thatcher favoured Major in the leadership contest, but her support for him waned in later years.<sup id="cite_ref-281" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-281">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_life">Later life</span></h2>
<p>Thatcher returned to the <a href="/wiki/Backbenches" class="mw-redirect" title="Backbenches">backbenches</a> as a constituency parliamentarian after leaving the premiership.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReitan2003118_282-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReitan2003118-282">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup> Her domestic approval rating recovered after her resignation; the balance of public opinion was that her government had been good for the country.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrewe1991_263-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrewe1991-263">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup> Aged 66, she retired from the House at the 1992 general election, saying that leaving the Commons would allow her more freedom to speak her mind.<sup id="cite_ref-lords_284-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lords-284">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Post-Commons:_1992.E2.80.932003"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-Commons:_1992–2003">Post-Commons: 1992–2003</span></h3>
<p>Upon leaving the House of Commons, Thatcher became the first former Prime Minister to set up a foundation;<sup id="cite_ref-285" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-285">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup> the British wing of the Margaret Thatcher Foundation was dissolved in 2005 due to financial difficulties.<sup id="cite_ref-286" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-286">&#91;281&#93;</a></sup> She wrote two volumes of memoirs, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Downing_Street_Years" title="The Downing Street Years">The Downing Street Years</a></i> (1993) and <a href="/wiki/The_Path_to_Power_(Margaret_Thatcher)" title="The Path to Power (Margaret Thatcher)"><i>The Path to Power</i></a> (1995). In 1991 she and her husband Denis moved to a house in <a href="/wiki/Chester_Square" title="Chester Square">Chester Square</a>, a residential garden square in central London's <a href="/wiki/Belgravia" title="Belgravia">Belgravia</a> district.<sup id="cite_ref-287" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-287">&#91;282&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thatcher was hired by the tobacco company <a href="/wiki/Altria" title="Altria">Philip Morris</a> as a "geopolitical consultant" in July 1992, for $250,000 per year and an annual contribution of $250,000 to her foundation.<sup id="cite_ref-288" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-288">&#91;283&#93;</a></sup> Thatcher earned $50,000 for each speech she delivered.<sup id="cite_ref-289" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-289">&#91;284&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In August 1992 she called for NATO to stop the Serbian assault on <a href="/wiki/Gora%C5%BEde" title="Goražde">Goražde</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sarajevo" title="Sarajevo">Sarajevo</a> to end <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_cleansing" title="Ethnic cleansing">ethnic cleansing</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Bosnian_War" title="Bosnian War">Bosnian War</a>. In an <a href="/wiki/Op-ed" title="Op-ed">op-ed</a>, she compared the situation in <a href="/wiki/Bosnia%E2%80%93Herzegovina" class="mw-redirect" title="Bosnia–Herzegovina">Bosnia–Herzegovina</a> to "the worst excesses of the Nazis", and warned that there could be a "holocaust".<sup id="cite_ref-290" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-290">&#91;285&#93;</a></sup> She was an advocate of <a href="/wiki/Independence_of_Croatia" title="Independence of Croatia">Croatian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Independence_of_Slovenia" class="mw-redirect" title="Independence of Slovenia">Slovenian</a> independence.<sup id="cite_ref-291" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-291">&#91;286&#93;</a></sup> In a 1991 interview for <a href="/wiki/Croatian_Radiotelevision" title="Croatian Radiotelevision">Croatian Radiotelevision</a>, Thatcher commented on the <a href="/wiki/Yugoslav_Wars" title="Yugoslav Wars">Yugoslav Wars</a>; she was critical of Western governments for not recognising the breakaway republics of Croatia and Slovenia as independent states and for not supplying them with armaments after the Serbian-led <a href="/wiki/Yugoslav_Army" class="mw-redirect" title="Yugoslav Army">Yugoslav Army</a> attacked.<sup id="cite_ref-292" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-292">&#91;287&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>She made a series of speeches in the Lords criticising the <a href="/wiki/Maastricht_Treaty" title="Maastricht Treaty">Maastricht Treaty</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-lords_284-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lords-284">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup> describing it as "a treaty too far" and stated: "I could never have signed this treaty."<sup id="cite_ref-293" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-293">&#91;288&#93;</a></sup> She cited <a href="/wiki/A._V._Dicey" title="A. V. Dicey">A. V. Dicey</a> when arguing that, as all three main parties were in favour of the treaty, the people should have their say in a referendum.<sup id="cite_ref-294" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-294">&#91;289&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thatcher served as honorary <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_(education)" title="Chancellor (education)">chancellor</a> of the <a href="/wiki/College_of_William_%26_Mary" title="College of William &amp; Mary">College of William &amp; Mary</a> in Virginia from 1993 to 2000,<sup id="cite_ref-295" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-295">&#91;290&#93;</a></sup> while also serving as chancellor of the private <a href="/wiki/University_of_Buckingham" title="University of Buckingham">University of Buckingham</a> from 1992 to 1998,<sup id="cite_ref-296" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-296">&#91;291&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kealey_297-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kealey-297">&#91;292&#93;</a></sup> a university she had formally opened in 1976 as the former Education Secretary.<sup id="cite_ref-Kealey_297-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kealey-297">&#91;292&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)_leadership_election,_1994" title="Labour Party (UK) leadership election, 1994">election as Labour Party leader</a> in 1994, Thatcher praised Blair as "probably the most formidable <a href="/wiki/Leader_of_the_Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Leader of the Labour Party (UK)">Labour leader</a> since <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Gaitskell" title="Hugh Gaitskell">Hugh Gaitskell</a>", adding: "I see a lot of socialism behind their front bench, but not in Mr Blair. I think he genuinely has moved."<sup id="cite_ref-298" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-298">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup> Blair responded in kind: "She was a thoroughly determined person, and that is an admirable quality".<sup id="cite_ref-299" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-299">&#91;294&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1998, Thatcher called for the release of former Chilean dictator <a href="/wiki/Augusto_Pinochet" title="Augusto Pinochet">Augusto Pinochet</a> when <a href="/wiki/Indictment_and_arrest_of_Augusto_Pinochet" title="Indictment and arrest of Augusto Pinochet">Spain had him arrested</a> and sought to try him for human rights violations.
