She cited the help he gave Britain during the Falklands War.<sup id="cite_ref-300" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-300">&#91;295&#93;</a></sup> In 1999, she visited him while he was under house arrest near London.<sup id="cite_ref-301" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-301">&#91;296&#93;</a></sup> Pinochet was released in March 2000 on medical grounds by Home Secretary <a href="/wiki/Jack_Straw" title="Jack Straw">Jack Straw</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-302" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-302">&#91;297&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Margaret_Thatcher.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Photograph" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Margaret_Thatcher.jpg/170px-Margaret_Thatcher.jpg" width="170" height="182" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Margaret_Thatcher.jpg/255px-Margaret_Thatcher.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Margaret_Thatcher.jpg/340px-Margaret_Thatcher.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1360" data-file-height="1452" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Margaret_Thatcher.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thatcher touring the <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center" title="Kennedy Space Center">Kennedy Space Center</a> in early 2001</div></div></div>
<p>At the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2001" title="United Kingdom general election, 2001">2001 general election</a>, Thatcher supported the Conservative campaign, as she had done in 1992 and 1997, and in the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)_leadership_election,_2001" title="Conservative Party (UK) leadership election, 2001">Conservative leadership election</a> following its defeat, she endorsed <a href="/wiki/Iain_Duncan_Smith" title="Iain Duncan Smith">Iain Duncan Smith</a> over <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Clarke" title="Kenneth Clarke">Kenneth Clarke</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-303" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-303">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup> In 2002 she encouraged <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> to aggressively tackle the "unfinished business" of <a href="/wiki/Ba%27athist_Iraq" title="Ba&#39;athist Iraq">Iraq under Saddam Hussein</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Thatcher_NYT_304-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thatcher_NYT-304">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup> and praised Blair for his "strong, bold leadership" in standing with Bush in the <a href="/wiki/Iraq_War" title="Iraq War">Iraq War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Thatcher_Blair_Iraq_305-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thatcher_Blair_Iraq-305">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>She broached the same subject in her <i><a href="/wiki/Statecraft:_Strategies_for_a_Changing_World" title="Statecraft: Strategies for a Changing World">Statecraft: Strategies for a Changing World</a></i>, which was published in April 2002 and dedicated to <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a>, writing that there would be no peace in the Middle East until <a href="/wiki/Saddam_Hussein" title="Saddam Hussein">Saddam Hussein</a> was toppled. Her book also said that Israel must trade <a href="/wiki/Land_for_peace" title="Land for peace">land for peace</a>, and that the European Union (EU) was a "fundamentally unreformable", "classic utopian project, a monument to the vanity of intellectuals, a programme whose inevitable destiny is failure".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGloverEconomides201020_306-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGloverEconomides201020-306">&#91;301&#93;</a></sup> She argued that Britain should renegotiate its terms of membership or else <a href="/wiki/Brexit" title="Brexit">leave the EU</a> and join the <a href="/wiki/North_American_Free_Trade_Agreement" title="North American Free Trade Agreement">North American Free Trade Area</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-307" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-307">&#91;302&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Following several small strokes she was advised by her doctors not to engage in further public speaking.<sup id="cite_ref-308" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-308">&#91;303&#93;</a></sup> In March 2002 she announced that on doctors' advice she would cancel all planned speaking engagements and accept no more.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell2003796–798_309-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell2003796–798-309">&#91;304&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:25em;&#32;; background-color: #f2f2f2;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303512">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{margin:0.5em auto 0.8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft p,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright p{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:larger;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:360px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{min-width:100%;margin:0 0 0.8em!important;float:none!important}}</style>
<div class="quotebox-title" style="background-color: #f2f2f2;">Quote from <i><a href="/wiki/The_Downing_Street_Years" title="The Downing Street Years">The Downing Street Years</a></i></div>
<div class="quotebox-quote quoted left-aligned" style="">Being Prime Minister is a lonely job. In a sense, it ought to be: you cannot lead from the crowd. But with Denis there I was never alone. What a man. What a husband. What a friend.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThatcher199323_310-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThatcher199323-310">&#91;305&#93;</a></sup></div>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">Thatcher on her husband Denis,&#32;published 1993</cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>On 26 June 2003, Thatcher's husband <a href="/wiki/Denis_Thatcher" title="Denis Thatcher">Sir Denis</a> died of <a href="/wiki/Pancreatic_cancer" title="Pancreatic cancer">pancreatic cancer</a>, and was cremated on 3 July.<sup id="cite_ref-311" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-311">&#91;306&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-312" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-312">&#91;307&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Final_years:_2003.E2.80.932013"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_years:_2003–2013">Final years: 2003–2013</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mulroney_Thatcher_and_Gorbachev_at_Reagan%27s_funeral.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Photograph" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Mulroney_Thatcher_and_Gorbachev_at_Reagan%27s_funeral.jpg/220px-Mulroney_Thatcher_and_Gorbachev_at_Reagan%27s_funeral.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Mulroney_Thatcher_and_Gorbachev_at_Reagan%27s_funeral.jpg/330px-Mulroney_Thatcher_and_Gorbachev_at_Reagan%27s_funeral.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Mulroney_Thatcher_and_Gorbachev_at_Reagan%27s_funeral.jpg/440px-Mulroney_Thatcher_and_Gorbachev_at_Reagan%27s_funeral.jpg 2x" data-file-width="515" data-file-height="343" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mulroney_Thatcher_and_Gorbachev_at_Reagan%27s_funeral.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thatcher (right) greeted by her contemporaries on the world stage at Ronald Reagan's funeral in 2004</div></div></div>
<p>On 11 June 2004, Thatcher (against doctor's orders) attended the <a href="/wiki/Death_and_state_funeral_of_Ronald_Reagan" title="Death and state funeral of Ronald Reagan">state funeral service for Ronald Reagan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-313" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-313">&#91;308&#93;</a></sup> She delivered her eulogy via videotape; in view of her health, the message had been pre-recorded several months earlier.<sup id="cite_ref-314" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-314">&#91;309&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-315" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-315">&#91;310&#93;</a></sup> Thatcher flew to <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a> with the Reagan entourage, and attended the memorial service and interment ceremony for the president at the <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan_Presidential_Library" title="Ronald Reagan Presidential Library">Ronald Reagan Presidential Library</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-316" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-316">&#91;311&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2005, Thatcher criticised the way the decision to <a href="/wiki/2003_invasion_of_Iraq" title="2003 invasion of Iraq">invade Iraq</a> had been made two years previously. Although she still supported the intervention to topple Saddam Hussein, she said that (as a scientist) she would always look for "facts, evidence and proof", before committing the armed forces.<sup id="cite_ref-grice_234-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grice-234">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> She celebrated her 80th birthday on 13 October at the <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_Oriental_Hyde_Park,_London" title="Mandarin Oriental Hyde Park, London">Mandarin Oriental Hotel</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hyde_Park,_London" title="Hyde Park, London">Hyde Park, London</a>; guests included the Queen, the <a href="/wiki/Prince_Philip,_Duke_of_Edinburgh" title="Prince Philip, Duke of Edinburgh">Duke of Edinburgh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Princess_Alexandra,_The_Honourable_Lady_Ogilvy" title="Princess Alexandra, The Honourable Lady Ogilvy">Princess Alexandra</a> and Tony Blair.<sup id="cite_ref-317" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-317">&#91;312&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Howe" title="Geoffrey Howe">Lord (Geoffrey) Howe of Aberavon</a> was also in attendance and said of Thatcher: "Her real triumph was to have transformed not just one party but two, so that when Labour did eventually return, the great bulk of Thatcherism was accepted as irreversible."<sup id="cite_ref-318" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-318">&#91;313&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thatcher's daughter Carol <a href="https://en.wikinews.org/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher_suffering_from_dementia,_says_daughter" class="extiw" title="n:Margaret Thatcher suffering from dementia, says daughter">first revealed</a> that her mother had <a href="/wiki/Dementia" title="Dementia">dementia</a> in 2005,<sup id="cite_ref-Langley_319-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Langley-319">&#91;314&#93;</a></sup> saying "Mum doesn't read much any more because of her memory loss". In her 2008 memoir, Carol wrote that her mother "could hardly remember the beginning of a sentence by the time she got to the end".<sup id="cite_ref-Langley_319-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Langley-319">&#91;314&#93;</a></sup> She later recounted how she was first struck by her mother's dementia when, in conversation, Thatcher confused the Falklands and Yugoslav conflicts; she recalled the pain of needing to tell her mother repeatedly that her husband Denis was dead.<sup id="cite_ref-320" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-320">&#91;315&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div style="clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;background-color:transparent">Thatcher in the US, 2006</div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Thatcher_2006_September_11_event.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Vice President Dick Cheney and Lynne Cheney stand with former Prime Minister Margaret Thatcher of Great Britain for a moment of silence on the South Lawn September 11, 2006, to commemorate the fifth anniversary of the September 11th terrorist attacks." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Thatcher_2006_September_11_event.jpg/200px-Thatcher_2006_September_11_event.jpg" width="200" height="133" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Thatcher_2006_September_11_event.jpg/300px-Thatcher_2006_September_11_event.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Thatcher_2006_September_11_event.jpg/400px-Thatcher_2006_September_11_event.jpg 2x" data-file-width="515" data-file-height="343" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Thatcher (left) at a <a href="/wiki/Patriot_Day" title="Patriot Day">Washington memorial service</a> on the fifth anniversary of the <a href="/wiki/9/11_attacks" class="mw-redirect" title="9/11 attacks">9/11 attacks</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Margaret_Thatcher_060912-F-0193C-006.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Margaret Thatcher, former Prime Minister of the United Kingdom, visits the Pentagon and shares a laugh with Secretary of Defense Donald H. Rumsfeld and Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff Marine Gen. Peter Pace." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Margaret_Thatcher_060912-F-0193C-006.jpg/200px-Margaret_Thatcher_060912-F-0193C-006.jpg" width="200" height="135" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Margaret_Thatcher_060912-F-0193C-006.jpg/300px-Margaret_Thatcher_060912-F-0193C-006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Margaret_Thatcher_060912-F-0193C-006.jpg/400px-Margaret_Thatcher_060912-F-0193C-006.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4065" data-file-height="2737" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Thatcher with <a href="/wiki/Donald_Rumsfeld" title="Donald Rumsfeld">Donald Rumsfeld</a> and <a href="/wiki/General_Pace" class="mw-redirect" title="General Pace">General Pace</a> at <a href="/wiki/The_Pentagon" title="The Pentagon">the Pentagon</a></div></div></div></div>
<p>In 2006, Thatcher attended the official Washington, D.C. memorial service to commemorate the fifth anniversary of the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">11 September attacks</a> on the US. She was a guest of Vice President <a href="/wiki/Dick_Cheney" title="Dick Cheney">Dick Cheney</a>, and met Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/Condoleezza_Rice" title="Condoleezza Rice">Condoleezza Rice</a> during her visit.<sup id="cite_ref-321" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-321">&#91;316&#93;</a></sup> In February 2007 Thatcher became the first <a href="/wiki/Living_Prime_Ministers_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Living Prime Ministers of the United Kingdom">living British prime minister</a> to be honoured with a <a href="/wiki/Statue_of_Margaret_Thatcher_(Palace_of_Westminster)" title="Statue of Margaret Thatcher (Palace of Westminster)">statue in the Houses of Parliament</a>. The bronze statue stands opposite that of her political hero, <a href="/wiki/Statue_of_Winston_Churchill,_Palace_of_Westminster" title="Statue of Winston Churchill, Palace of Westminster">Sir Winston Churchill</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-bronze_322-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bronze-322">&#91;317&#93;</a></sup> and was unveiled on 21 February 2007 with Thatcher in attendance; she remarked in the <a href="/wiki/Members%27_Lobby" title="Members&#39; Lobby">Members' Lobby</a> of the Commons: "I might have preferred iron&#160;&#8211;&#32;but bronze will do&#160;... It won't rust."<sup id="cite_ref-bronze_322-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bronze-322">&#91;317&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thatcher was a public supporter of the <a href="/wiki/Prague_Declaration_on_European_Conscience_and_Communism" title="Prague Declaration on European Conscience and Communism">Prague Declaration on European Conscience and Communism</a> and the resulting Prague Process, and sent a public letter of support to its preceding conference.<sup id="cite_ref-323" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-323">&#91;318&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After collapsing at a <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a> dinner, Thatcher, suffering <a href="/wiki/Low_blood_pressure" class="mw-redirect" title="Low blood pressure">low blood pressure</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-324" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-324">&#91;319&#93;</a></sup> was admitted to <a href="/wiki/St_Thomas%27_Hospital" title="St Thomas&#39; Hospital">St Thomas' Hospital</a> in central London on 7 March 2008 for tests. In 2009 she was hospitalised again when she fell and broke her arm.<sup id="cite_ref-325" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-325">&#91;320&#93;</a></sup> Thatcher returned to 10 Downing Street in late November 2009 for the unveiling of an official portrait by artist <a href="/wiki/Richard_Stone_(painter)" title="Richard Stone (painter)">Richard Stone</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-ThatcherReturns_326-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ThatcherReturns-326">&#91;321&#93;</a></sup> an unusual honour for a living former Prime Minister. Stone was previously commissioned to paint portraits of the Queen and <a href="/wiki/Queen_Mother" class="mw-redirect" title="Queen Mother">Queen Mother</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ThatcherReturns_326-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ThatcherReturns-326">&#91;321&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 4 July 2011, Thatcher was to attend a ceremony for the unveiling of a 10&#160;ft (3.0&#160;m) statue to Ronald Reagan, outside the <a href="/wiki/Embassy_of_the_United_States,_London" title="Embassy of the United States, London">US Embassy</a> in London, but was unable to attend due to her frail health.<sup id="cite_ref-327" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-327">&#91;322&#93;</a></sup> She last attended a sitting of the House of Lords on 19 July 2010,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUK_Parliament2013_328-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUK_Parliament2013-328">&#91;323&#93;</a></sup> and on 30 July 2011 it was announced that her office in the Lords had been closed.<sup id="cite_ref-329" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-329">&#91;324&#93;</a></sup> Earlier that month, Thatcher was named the most competent Prime Minister of the past 30 years in an <a href="/wiki/Ipsos_MORI" title="Ipsos MORI">Ipsos MORI</a> poll.<sup id="cite_ref-330" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-330">&#91;325&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_and_funeral:_2013">Death and funeral: 2013</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Death_and_funeral_of_Margaret_Thatcher" title="Death and funeral of Margaret Thatcher">Death and funeral of Margaret Thatcher</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Margaret_Thatcher_coffin_at_St._Pauls_X8A2604.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Photograph" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Margaret_Thatcher_coffin_at_St._Pauls_X8A2604.jpg/220px-Margaret_Thatcher_coffin_at_St._Pauls_X8A2604.jpg" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Margaret_Thatcher_coffin_at_St._Pauls_X8A2604.jpg/330px-Margaret_Thatcher_coffin_at_St._Pauls_X8A2604.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Margaret_Thatcher_coffin_at_St._Pauls_X8A2604.jpg/440px-Margaret_Thatcher_coffin_at_St._Pauls_X8A2604.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3060" data-file-height="1959" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Margaret_Thatcher_coffin_at_St._Pauls_X8A2604.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thatcher's coffin being carried up the steps of <a href="/wiki/St_Paul%27s_Cathedral" title="St Paul&#39;s Cathedral">St Paul's Cathedral</a></div></div></div>
<p>Baroness Thatcher died on 8 April 2013, at the age of 87, after suffering a stroke. She had been staying at a suite in <a href="/wiki/The_Ritz_Hotel,_London" title="The Ritz Hotel, London">the Ritz Hotel</a> in London since December 2012 after having difficulty with stairs at her <a href="/wiki/Chester_Square" title="Chester Square">Chester Square</a> home in <a href="/wiki/Belgravia" title="Belgravia">Belgravia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-331" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-331">&#91;326&#93;</a></sup> Her death certificate listed the primary causes of death as a "cerebrovascular accident" and "repeated <a href="/wiki/Transient_ischaemic_attack" class="mw-redirect" title="Transient ischaemic attack">transient ischaemic attack</a>";<sup id="cite_ref-Mason_332-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mason-332">&#91;327&#93;</a></sup> secondary causes were listed as a "<a href="/wiki/Carcinoma" title="Carcinoma">carcinoma</a> of the bladder" and dementia.<sup id="cite_ref-Mason_332-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mason-332">&#91;327&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="https://en.wikiquote.org/wiki/Death_and_funeral_of_Margaret_Thatcher" class="extiw" title="q:Death and funeral of Margaret Thatcher">Reactions to the news of Thatcher's death</a> were mixed across the UK, ranging from tributes lauding her as Britain's greatest-ever peacetime Prime Minister to public celebrations of her death and expressions of hatred and personalised vitriol.<sup id="cite_ref-333" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-333">&#91;328&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:MTgrave1.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="Photograph" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/MTgrave1.jpeg/220px-MTgrave1.jpeg" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/MTgrave1.jpeg/330px-MTgrave1.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/MTgrave1.jpeg/440px-MTgrave1.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1936" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:MTgrave1.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Graves of Margaret and Denis Thatcher at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Hospital_Chelsea" title="Royal Hospital Chelsea">Royal Hospital Chelsea</a></div></div></div>
<p>Details of Thatcher's funeral had been agreed with her in advance.<sup id="cite_ref-Independent,_12_April_2013_334-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Independent,_12_April_2013-334">&#91;329&#93;</a></sup> She received a <a href="/wiki/State_funerals_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="State funerals in the United Kingdom">ceremonial funeral</a>, including full military honours, with a church service at <a href="/wiki/St_Paul%27s_Cathedral" title="St Paul&#39;s Cathedral">St Paul's Cathedral</a> on 17 April.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_335-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-335">&#91;330&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BBC_News,_9_April_2013_336-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_News,_9_April_2013-336">&#91;331&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Queen Elizabeth II and the Duke of Edinburgh attended her funeral,<sup id="cite_ref-337" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-337">&#91;332&#93;</a></sup> marking only the second time in the Queen's reign that she attended the funeral of any of <a href="/wiki/List_of_prime_ministers_of_Queen_Elizabeth_II" class="mw-redirect" title="List of prime ministers of Queen Elizabeth II">her former prime ministers</a>; the first and only precedent being that of <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a>, who received a state funeral in 1965.<sup id="cite_ref-338" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-338">&#91;333&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After the service at St Paul's Cathedral, Thatcher's body was cremated at <a href="/wiki/Mortlake_Crematorium" title="Mortlake Crematorium">Mortlake Crematorium</a>, where her husband had been cremated. On 28 September, a service for Thatcher was held in the All Saints Chapel of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Hospital_Chelsea" title="Royal Hospital Chelsea">Royal Hospital Chelsea</a>'s Margaret Thatcher Infirmary. In a private ceremony, Thatcher's ashes were interred in the grounds of the hospital, next to those of her husband.<sup id="cite_ref-339" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-339">&#91;334&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BBC-intere_340-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-intere-340">&#91;335&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>

<li><a href="/wiki/File:Royal_Coat_of_Arms_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg" class="image"><img alt="Royal Coat of Arms of the United Kingdom.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Royal_Coat_of_Arms_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/16px-Royal_Coat_of_Arms_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png" width="16" height="15" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Royal_Coat_of_Arms_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/24px-Royal_Coat_of_Arms_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Royal_Coat_of_Arms_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/32px-Royal_Coat_of_Arms_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1550" data-file-height="1500" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:British_politics" title="Portal:British politics">British politics&#32;portal</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/File:Emblem-money.svg" class="image"><img alt="Emblem-money.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Emblem-money.svg/16px-Emblem-money.svg.png" width="16" height="16" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Emblem-money.svg/24px-Emblem-money.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Emblem-money.svg/32px-Emblem-money.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="48" data-file-height="48" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Business_and_economics" title="Portal:Business and economics">Business and economics&#32;portal</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/File:2006_AEGold_Proof_Obv.png" class="image"><img alt="2006 AEGold Proof Obv.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/2006_AEGold_Proof_Obv.png/16px-2006_AEGold_Proof_Obv.png" width="16" height="16" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/2006_AEGold_Proof_Obv.png/24px-2006_AEGold_Proof_Obv.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/2006_AEGold_Proof_Obv.png/32px-2006_AEGold_Proof_Obv.png 2x" data-file-width="1770" data-file-height="1762" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Libertarianism" title="Portal:Libertarianism">Libertarianism&#32;portal</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Thatcherism" title="Template:Thatcherism"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Thatcherism" title="Template talk:Thatcherism"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Thatcherism&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Thatcherism represented a systematic and decisive overhaul of the <a href="/wiki/Post-war_consensus" title="Post-war consensus">post-war consensus</a>, whereby the major political parties largely agreed on the central themes of <a href="/wiki/Keynesianism" class="mw-redirect" title="Keynesianism">Keynesianism</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Welfare_state_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Welfare state in the United Kingdom">welfare state</a>, nationalised industry, and close regulation of the economy, and high taxes. Thatcher generally supported the welfare state, while proposing to rid it of abuses.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore201387_341-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore201387-341">&#91;336&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>She promised in 1982 that the highly popular <a href="/wiki/National_Health_Service" title="National Health Service">National Health Service</a> was "safe in our hands".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKlein1985_342-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlein1985-342">&#91;337&#93;</a></sup> At first she ignored the question of privatising nationalised industries. Heavily influenced by right-wing think tanks, and especially by <a href="/wiki/Keith_Joseph" title="Keith Joseph">Keith Joseph</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarr2007358_343-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarr2007358-343">&#91;338&#93;</a></sup> Thatcher broadened her attack. Thatcherism came to refer to her policies as well as aspects of her ethical outlook and personal style, including <a href="/wiki/Moral_absolutism" title="Moral absolutism">moral absolutism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nationalism" title="Nationalism">nationalism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Individualism" title="Individualism">interest in the individual</a>, and an uncompromising approach to achieving political goals.<sup id="cite_ref-eb_344-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eb-344">&#91;339&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-345" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-345">&#91;340&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-347" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-347">&#91;nb 6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>
Thatcher defined her own political philosophy in a major and controversial break with the <a href="/wiki/One-nation_conservatism" title="One-nation conservatism">one-nation conservatism</a> of <a href="/wiki/Edward_Heath" title="Edward Heath">Edward Heath</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell2011530–532_348-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell2011530–532-348">&#91;342&#93;</a></sup> and her Conservative predecessors in an interview published in <i><a href="/wiki/Woman%27s_Own" title="Woman&#39;s Own">Woman's Own</a></i> magazine, three months after her victory in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1987" title="United Kingdom general election, 1987">1987 general election</a>:</p><blockquote><p>I think we have gone through a period when too many children and people have been given to understand "I have a problem, it is the Government's job to cope with it!" or "I have a problem, I will go and get a grant to cope with it!" "I am homeless, the Government must house me!" and so they are casting their problems on society and who is society? There is no such thing! There are individual men and women and there are families and no government can do anything except through people and people look to themselves first. It is our duty to look after ourselves and then also to help look after our neighbour and life is a reciprocal business and people have got the entitlements too much in mind without the obligations.<sup id="cite_ref-society_349-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-society-349">&#91;343&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-350" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-350">&#91;nb 7&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span></h4>
<p>The number of adults owning shares rose from 7 per cent to 25 per cent during her tenure, and more than a million families bought their council houses, giving an increase from 55 per cent to 67 per cent in owner occupiers from 1979 to 1990. The houses were sold at a discount of 33–55 per cent, leading to large profits for some new owners. Personal wealth rose by 80 per cent in real terms during the 1980s, mainly due to rising house prices and increased earnings. Shares in the privatised utilities were sold below their market value to ensure quick and wide sales, rather than maximise national income.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarr2007430_351-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarr2007430-351">&#91;344&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-352" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-352">&#91;345&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thatcher's premiership was also marked by periods of high unemployment and social unrest,<sup id="cite_ref-legacy-bbc_353-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-legacy-bbc-353">&#91;346&#93;</a></sup> and many critics on the left of the political spectrum fault her economic policies for the unemployment level; many of the areas affected by mass unemployment as well as her <a href="/wiki/Monetarism" title="Monetarism">monetarist</a> economic policies remained blighted for decades, by such social problems as <a href="/wiki/Drug_abuse" class="mw-redirect" title="Drug abuse">drug abuse</a> and family breakdown.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERichards200463_354-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERichards200463-354">&#91;347&#93;</a></sup> Unemployment did not fall below its 1979 level during her tenure,<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_News,_8_April_2013_124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_News,_8_April_2013-124">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> although in June 1990 the recorded rate (5.4%) was lower than the rate in April 1979 (5.5%).<sup id="cite_ref-355" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-355">&#91;348&#93;</a></sup> The long-term effects of her policies on manufacturing remain contentious.<sup id="cite_ref-356" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-356">&#91;349&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell201179_357-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell201179-357">&#91;350&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Conversing in Scotland in April 2009, Thatcher insisted she had no regrets and was right to introduce the "<a href="/wiki/Poll_tax_(Great_Britain)" title="Poll tax (Great Britain)">poll tax</a>" and withdraw subsidies from "outdated industries, whose markets were in terminal decline", subsidies that created "the culture of dependency, which had done such damage to Britain".<sup id="cite_ref-358" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-358">&#91;351&#93;</a></sup> Political economist <a href="/wiki/Susan_Strange" title="Susan Strange">Susan Strange</a> termed the new financial growth model "casino capitalism", reflecting her view that speculation and financial trading were becoming more important to the economy than industry.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGamble200916_359-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGamble200916-359">&#91;352&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Critics on the left describe her as divisive<sup id="cite_ref-greatestpm_360-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greatestpm-360">&#91;353&#93;</a></sup> and claim she condoned greed and selfishness.<sup id="cite_ref-legacy-bbc_353-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-legacy-bbc-353">&#91;346&#93;</a></sup> Welsh politician <a href="/wiki/Rhodri_Morgan" title="Rhodri Morgan">Rhodri Morgan</a><sup id="cite_ref-361" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-361">&#91;354&#93;</a></sup> and others<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWest2012176Blundell201388_362-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest2012176Blundell201388-362">&#91;355&#93;</a></sup> have characterised Thatcher as a "<a href="/wiki/Marmite" title="Marmite">Marmite</a>" figure. <a href="/wiki/Michael_White_(journalist)" title="Michael White (journalist)">Michael White</a>, writing in the <i><a href="/wiki/New_Statesman" title="New Statesman">New Statesman</a></i>, challenged the view that her reforms had brought a net benefit.<sup id="cite_ref-363" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-363">&#91;356&#93;</a></sup> Others depict her approach as having been "a mixed bag"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERothbard1995chpt._63_364-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERothbard1995chpt._63-364">&#91;357&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-365" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-365">&#91;358&#93;</a></sup> or "<a href="/wiki/Curate%27s_egg" title="Curate&#39;s egg">Curate's egg</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnson1991chpt._8_366-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnson1991chpt._8-366">&#91;359&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thatcher did "little to advance the political cause of women" either within her party or the government.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans200425_367-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans200425-367">&#91;360&#93;</a></sup> Burns states that some <a href="/wiki/Feminism_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Feminism in the United Kingdom">British feminists</a> regarded her as "an enemy".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurns2009234_368-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurns2009234-368">&#91;361&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/June_Purvis" title="June Purvis">June Purvis</a> claims that although Thatcher had struggled laboriously against the sexist prejudices of her day to rise to the top, she made no effort to ease the path for other women.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPurvis2013_369-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPurvis2013-369">&#91;362&#93;</a></sup> Thatcher did not regard <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_rights" title="Women&#39;s rights">women's rights</a> as requiring particular attention as she did not, especially during her premiership, consider that women were being deprived of their rights. She suggested that women should be shortlisted by default for all public appointments but had once proposed that those with young children ought to leave the work force.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGelb198958–59_370-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGelb198958–59-370">&#91;363&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thatcher's stance on <a href="/wiki/Modern_immigration_to_the_United_Kingdom" title="Modern immigration to the United Kingdom">immigration</a> in the late 1970s was perceived as part of a rising racist public discourse,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitte201454_371-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitte201454-371">&#91;364&#93;</a></sup> which film critic Martin Barker termed "<a href="/wiki/New_racism" title="New racism">new racism</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChin200992Barker1981_372-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChin200992Barker1981-372">&#91;365&#93;</a></sup> As Leader of the Opposition, Thatcher believed that the <a href="/wiki/National_Front_(UK)" title="National Front (UK)">National Front</a> was winning over large numbers of Conservative voters with warnings against floods of immigrants. Her strategy was to undermine the Front narrative by acknowledging that many of their voters had serious concerns in need of addressing. In January 1978, Thatcher criticised Labour immigration policy with the goal of attracting voters away from the Front and to the Conservatives.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWitte201453–54_373-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWitte201453–54-373">&#91;366&#93;</a></sup> Her rhetoric was followed by an increase in Conservative support at the expense of the Front. Critics on the left reacted in accusing her of <a href="/wiki/Pandering_(politics)" title="Pandering (politics)">pandering</a> to racism.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFriedman200613_374-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFriedman200613-374">&#91;367&#93;</a></sup> Sociologists Mark Mitchell and Dave Russell responded that Thatcher had been badly misinterpreted, arguing that <a href="/wiki/Race_(human_classification)" class="mw-redirect" title="Race (human classification)">race</a> was never an important focus of Thatcherism.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMitchellRussell1989_375-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMitchellRussell1989-375">&#91;368&#93;</a></sup> Throughout her premiership, both major parties took similar positions on immigration policy,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWard2004128Vinen2009227,_279_376-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWard2004128Vinen2009227,_279-376">&#91;369&#93;</a></sup> having in 1981 passed the <a href="/wiki/British_Nationality_Act_1981" title="British Nationality Act 1981">British Nationality Act</a> with bipartisan support.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHansen2000207–208_377-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHansen2000207–208-377">&#91;370&#93;</a></sup> There were no policies passed or proposed by her government aimed at restricting immigration, and the subject of race was never highlighted by Thatcher in any of her major speeches as Prime Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnwar2001_378-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnwar2001-378">&#91;371&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many of Thatcher's policies had an influence on the Labour Party,<sup id="cite_ref-379" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-379">&#91;372&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeldon200714_380-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeldon200714-380">&#91;373&#93;</a></sup> which had returned to power in 1997 under <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a>. Blair rebranded the party "<a href="/wiki/New_Labour" title="New Labour">New Labour</a>" in 1994 with an aim of increasing its appeal beyond its traditional supporters,<sup id="cite_ref-381" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-381">&#91;374&#93;</a></sup> and to attract those who had supported Thatcher, such as the "<a href="/wiki/Essex_man" title="Essex man">Essex man</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-382" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-382">&#91;375&#93;</a></sup> She is said to have regarded New Labour as her greatest achievement.<sup id="cite_ref-383" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-383">&#91;376&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Shortly after Thatcher's death, Scottish First Minister <a href="/wiki/Alex_Salmond" title="Alex Salmond">Alex Salmond</a> argued that her policies had the "unintended consequence" of encouraging <a href="/wiki/Scottish_devolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish devolution">Scottish devolution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-384" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-384">&#91;377&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lord_Foulkes_of_Cumnock" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Foulkes of Cumnock">Lord Foulkes of Cumnock</a> agreed on <i><a href="/wiki/Scotland_Tonight" title="Scotland Tonight">Scotland Tonight</a></i> that she had provided "the impetus" for devolution.<sup id="cite_ref-385" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-385">&#91;378&#93;</a></sup> Writing for <i><a href="/wiki/The_Scotsman" title="The Scotsman">The Scotsman</a></i>, Thatcher argued against devolution on the basis that it would eventually lead to <a href="/wiki/Scottish_independence" title="Scottish independence">Scottish independence</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-386" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-386">&#91;379&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Reputation">Reputation</span></h4>
<p>Thatcher's <a href="/wiki/List_of_Prime_Ministers_of_the_United_Kingdom_by_tenure" title="List of Prime Ministers of the United Kingdom by tenure">tenure of 11 years and 209 days</a> as Prime Minister was the longest since <a href="/wiki/Lord_Salisbury" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Salisbury">Lord Salisbury</a> (13 years and 252 days, in three spells) and the longest continuous period in office since <a href="/wiki/Lord_Liverpool" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Liverpool">Lord Liverpool</a> (14 years and 305 days).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGardinerThompson201312_387-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGardinerThompson201312-387">&#91;380&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-388" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-388">&#91;381&#93;</a></sup> She remains the longest-serving Prime Minister officially referred to as such, as the post was only officially given recognition in the <a href="/wiki/Orders_of_precedence_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Orders of precedence in the United Kingdom">order of precedence</a> in 1905.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarriott192383_389-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarriott192383-389">&#91;382&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Having led her party to general election victories three times in a row (twice in landslide), she ranks as the most popular party leader in British history in terms of votes cast for the winning party, with over 40 million ballots cast for the Conservatives in total between 1979 and 1987.<sup id="cite_ref-390" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-390">&#91;383&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-391" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-391">&#91;384&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-392" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-392">&#91;385&#93;</a></sup> Her final election win was hailed as a "historic <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/hat_trick" class="extiw" title="wikt:hat trick">hat trick</a>" by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Independent" title="The Independent">The Independent</a></i> and other newspapers.<sup id="cite_ref-393" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-393">&#91;386&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thatcher was voted the fourth-greatest British prime minister of the 20th century in a poll of 139 academics organised by <a href="/wiki/MORI" class="mw-redirect" title="MORI">MORI</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-394" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-394">&#91;387&#93;</a></sup> and in 2002 she ranked highest among living persons in the BBC poll <i><a href="/wiki/100_Greatest_Britons" title="100 Greatest Britons">100 Greatest Britons</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-395" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-395">&#91;388&#93;</a></sup> In 1999, <a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)"><i>Time</i></a> deemed Thatcher one of the <a href="/wiki/Time_100:_The_Most_Important_People_of_the_Century" title="Time 100: The Most Important People of the Century">100 most important people of the 20th century</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-396" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-396">&#91;389&#93;</a></sup> In 2015 she topped a poll by <i><a href="/wiki/Scottish_Widows" title="Scottish Widows">Scottish Widows</a></i>, a major financial services company, as the most influential woman of the past 200 years;<sup id="cite_ref-397" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-397">&#91;390&#93;</a></sup> and in 2016 topped <a href="/wiki/BBC_Radio_4" title="BBC Radio 4">BBC Radio 4</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Woman%27s_Hour" title="Woman&#39;s Hour">Woman's Hour</a> Power List</i> of women judged to have had the biggest impact on female lives over the past 70 years.<sup id="cite_ref-398" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-398">&#91;391&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-399" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-399">&#91;392&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_depictions">Cultural depictions</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cultural_depictions_of_Margaret_Thatcher" title="Cultural depictions of Margaret Thatcher">Cultural depictions of Margaret Thatcher</a></div>
<p>According to theatre critic <a href="/wiki/Michael_Billington_(critic)" title="Michael Billington (critic)">Michael Billington</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-400" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-400">&#91;393&#93;</a></sup> Thatcher left an "emphatic mark" on the arts while Prime Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-401" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-401">&#91;394&#93;</a></sup> One of the earliest satires of Thatcher as Prime Minister involved satirist <a href="/wiki/John_Wells_(satirist)" title="John Wells (satirist)">John Wells</a> (as writer and performer), actress <a href="/wiki/Janet_Brown" title="Janet Brown">Janet Brown</a> (voicing Thatcher) and future <i><a href="/wiki/Spitting_Image" title="Spitting Image">Spitting Image</a></i> producer <a href="/wiki/John_Lloyd_(producer)" title="John Lloyd (producer)">John Lloyd</a> (as co-producer), who in 1979 were teamed up by producer <a href="/wiki/Martin_Lewis_(humorist)" title="Martin Lewis (humorist)">Martin Lewis</a> for the satirical audio album <a href="/wiki/The_Iron_Lady_(album)" title="The Iron Lady (album)"><i>The Iron Lady</i></a>, which consisted of skits and songs satirising Thatcher's rise to power. The album was released in September 1979.<sup id="cite_ref-402" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-402">&#91;395&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-403" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-403">&#91;396&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thatcher was the subject or the inspiration for 1980s <a href="/wiki/Protest_song" title="Protest song">protest songs</a>. Musicians <a href="/wiki/Billy_Bragg" title="Billy Bragg">Billy Bragg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paul_Weller" title="Paul Weller">Paul Weller</a> helped to form the <a href="/wiki/Red_Wedge" title="Red Wedge">Red Wedge</a> collective to support Labour in opposition to Thatcher.<sup id="cite_ref-Songs_404-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Songs-404">&#91;397&#93;</a></sup> Known as "Maggie" by supporters and opponents alike, the chant song "<a href="/wiki/Maggie_Out" title="Maggie Out">Maggie Out</a>" became a signature rallying cry among the left during the latter half of her premiership.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVinen20091947–1948Barr2013178,_235_405-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVinen20091947–1948Barr2013178,_235-405">&#91;398&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thatcher was parodied by Wells in several media. He collaborated with <a href="/wiki/Richard_Ingrams" title="Richard Ingrams">Richard Ingrams</a> on the spoof "<a href="/wiki/Dear_Bill" title="Dear Bill">Dear Bill</a>" letters, which ran as a column in <i><a href="/wiki/Private_Eye" title="Private Eye">Private Eye</a></i> magazine; they were also published in book form and became a West End stage revue titled <i>Anyone for Denis?</i>, with Wells in the role of <a href="/wiki/Denis_Thatcher" title="Denis Thatcher">Denis Thatcher</a>. It was followed by a <a href="/wiki/Anyone_for_Denis%3F_(video)" title="Anyone for Denis? (video)">1982 TV special</a> directed by <a href="/wiki/Dick_Clement" title="Dick Clement">Dick Clement</a>, in which Thatcher was played by <a href="/wiki/Angela_Thorne" title="Angela Thorne">Angela Thorne</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-406" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-406">&#91;399&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Since her resignation as Prime Minister in 1990, Thatcher has been portrayed in a number of television programmes, documentaries, films and plays.<sup id="cite_ref-407" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-407">&#91;400&#93;</a></sup> She was portrayed by <a href="/wiki/Patricia_Hodge" title="Patricia Hodge">Patricia Hodge</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ian_Curteis" title="Ian Curteis">Ian Curteis</a>'s long unproduced <i><a href="/wiki/The_Falklands_Play" title="The Falklands Play">The Falklands Play</a></i> (2002) and by <a href="/wiki/Andrea_Riseborough" title="Andrea Riseborough">Andrea Riseborough</a> in the TV film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Long_Walk_to_Finchley" title="The Long Walk to Finchley">The Long Walk to Finchley</a></i> (2008). She is the protagonist in two films, played by <a href="/wiki/Lindsay_Duncan" title="Lindsay Duncan">Lindsay Duncan</a> in <a href="/wiki/Margaret_(2009_film)" title="Margaret (2009 film)"><i>Margaret</i></a> (2009) and by <a href="/wiki/Meryl_Streep" title="Meryl Streep">Meryl Streep</a> in <a href="/wiki/The_Iron_Lady_(film)" title="The Iron Lady (film)"><i>The Iron Lady</i></a> (2011),<sup id="cite_ref-408" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-408">&#91;401&#93;</a></sup> in which she is depicted as suffering from dementia or <a href="/wiki/Alzheimer%27s_disease" title="Alzheimer&#39;s disease">Alzheimer's disease</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-409" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-409">&#91;402&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Titles.2C_awards_and_honours"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Titles,_awards_and_honours">Titles, awards and honours</span></h2>
<p>Thatcher became a <a href="/wiki/Privy_Council_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Privy Council of the United Kingdom">Privy Councillor</a> (PC) upon becoming Secretary of State for Education and Science in 1970.<sup id="cite_ref-410" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-410">&#91;403&#93;</a></sup> She was the first woman entitled to full membership rights as an honorary member of the <a href="/wiki/Carlton_Club" title="Carlton Club">Carlton Club</a> on becoming Leader of the Conservative Party in 1975.<sup id="cite_ref-411" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-411">&#91;404&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As Prime Minister, Thatcher received two honorary distinctions:
</p>
<ul><li><span class="vevent">24&#160;October&#160;1979<span style="display:none">&#160;(<span class="bday dtstart published updated">1979-10-24</span>)</span>: <span class="summary">Honorary Fellowship (Hon.) of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Institute_of_Chemistry" title="Royal Institute of Chemistry">Royal Institute of Chemistry</a> (FRIC),<sup id="cite_ref-412" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-412">&#91;405&#93;</a></sup> which was merged into the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society_of_Chemistry" title="Royal Society of Chemistry">Royal Society of Chemistry</a> (FRSC) the following year;<sup id="cite_ref-413" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-413">&#91;406&#93;</a></sup></span></span></li>
<li><span class="vevent">1&#160;July&#160;1983<span style="display:none">&#160;(<span class="bday dtstart published updated">1983-07-01</span>)</span>: <span class="summary"><a href="/wiki/Fellow_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Fellow of the Royal Society">Fellowship of the Royal Society</a> (FRS), a point of controversy among some of the then-existing Fellows.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;New_Scientist&#39;&#39;1983_414-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;New_Scientist&#39;&#39;1983-414">&#91;407&#93;</a></sup></span></span></li></ul>
<p>Within two weeks of her resignation, Thatcher was appointed a Member of the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_Merit" title="Order of Merit">Order of Merit</a> (OM) by <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Queen Elizabeth II</a> in December 1990. Her husband Denis was honoured with a <a href="/wiki/Thatcher_baronets" title="Thatcher baronets">hereditary baronetcy</a> at the same time.<sup id="cite_ref-OMBarontecy_415-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OMBarontecy-415">&#91;408&#93;</a></sup> As the spouse of a knight, Thatcher was entitled to use the <a href="/wiki/Style_(manner_of_address)" title="Style (manner of address)">honorific style</a> "Lady",<sup id="cite_ref-416" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-416">&#91;409&#93;</a></sup> an automatically conferred title that she declined to use.<sup id="cite_ref-417" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-417">&#91;410&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-418" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-418">&#91;411&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-419" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-419">&#91;412&#93;</a></sup> She became Lady Thatcher in her own right in 1992,<sup id="cite_ref-Tuohy_420-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tuohy-420">&#91;413&#93;</a></sup> upon her ennoblement in the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Tuohy_420-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tuohy-420">&#91;413&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Margaret_Thatcher_awarded_Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Photograph" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Margaret_Thatcher_awarded_Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom.jpg/220px-Margaret_Thatcher_awarded_Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom.jpg" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Margaret_Thatcher_awarded_Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom.jpg/330px-Margaret_Thatcher_awarded_Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Margaret_Thatcher_awarded_Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom.jpg/440px-Margaret_Thatcher_awarded_Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="404" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Margaret_Thatcher_awarded_Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thatcher receiving the <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom" title="Presidential Medal of Freedom">Presidential Medal of Freedom</a> in 1991</div></div></div>

href="/wiki/Order_of_the_Garter" title="Order of the Garter">Order of the Garter</a> (1995)</div></div></div></div>
<p>Thatcher was awarded twice in 1991 with the highest civilian awards of the United States and South Africa respectively:
</p>
<ul><li><span class="vevent">7&#160;March&#160;1991<span style="display:none">&#160;(<span class="bday dtstart published updated">1991-03-07</span>)</span>: <span class="summary">the <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom" title="Presidential Medal of Freedom">Presidential Medal of Freedom</a> on behalf of President <a href="/wiki/George_H._W._Bush" title="George H. W. Bush">George H. W. Bush</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-421" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-421">&#91;414&#93;</a></sup></span></span></li>
<li><span class="vevent">15&#160;May&#160;1991<span style="display:none">&#160;(<span class="bday dtstart published updated">1991-05-15</span>)</span>: <span class="summary">the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_Good_Hope" title="Order of Good Hope">Grand Cross of the Order of Good Hope</a> on behalf of President <a href="/wiki/F._W._de_Klerk" title="F. W. de Klerk">F. W. de Klerk</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-422" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-422">&#91;415&#93;</a></sup></span></span></li></ul>
<p>She was appointed a <a href="/wiki/Order_of_Saint_John_(chartered_1888)" title="Order of Saint John (chartered 1888)">Dame of the Order of St John</a> (DStJ) in July 1991.<sup id="cite_ref-423" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-423">&#91;416&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the Falklands, <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher_Day" class="mw-redirect" title="Margaret Thatcher Day">Margaret Thatcher Day</a> has been marked each 10 January since 1992,<sup id="cite_ref-Reuters-January_424-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters-January-424">&#91;417&#93;</a></sup> commemorating her first visit to the Islands in January 1983, six months after the <a href="/wiki/Aftermath_of_the_Falklands_War" title="Aftermath of the Falklands War">end of the Falklands War</a> in June 1982.<sup id="cite_ref-425" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-425">&#91;418&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thatcher became a <a href="/wiki/Members_of_the_House_of_Lords" title="Members of the House of Lords">member of the House of Lords</a> in 1992 with a <a href="/wiki/Life_peer" title="Life peer">life peerage</a> as Baroness Thatcher, of Kesteven in the County of Lincolnshire.<sup id="cite_ref-lords_284-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lords-284">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-426" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-426">&#91;419&#93;</a></sup> As a peer, Thatcher was entitled to use a <a href="/wiki/List_of_coats_of_arms_of_Prime_Ministers_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="List of coats of arms of Prime Ministers of the United Kingdom">personal coat of arms</a>. A second coat of arms was created for Thatcher following her appointment as a <a href="/wiki/Lady_Companion_of_the_Order_of_the_Garter" class="mw-redirect" title="Lady Companion of the Order of the Garter">Lady Companion of the Order of the Garter</a> (LG) in 1995, the highest order of <a href="/wiki/Chivalry" title="Chivalry">chivalry</a> for women.<sup id="cite_ref-427" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-427">&#91;420&#93;</a></sup> Despite having received her own arms, Thatcher often used the <a href="/wiki/Royal_coat_of_arms_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Royal coat of arms of the United Kingdom">Royal Arms</a> instead of her own contrary to protocol.<sup id="cite_ref-428" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-428">&#91;421&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<p>In the US, Thatcher received the <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan_Freedom_Award" title="Ronald Reagan Freedom Award">Ronald Reagan Freedom Award</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-429" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-429">&#91;422&#93;</a></sup> and was designated Patron of <a href="/wiki/The_Heritage_Foundation" title="The Heritage Foundation">The Heritage Foundation</a> in 2006,<sup id="cite_ref-430" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-430">&#91;423&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-431" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-431">&#91;424&#93;</a></sup> where she established the Margaret Thatcher Center for Freedom.<sup id="cite_ref-432" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-432">&#91;425&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Styles_of_address">Styles of address</span></h2>
<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em;">
<ul><li>1925–1951: Miss Margaret Roberts</li>
<li>1951–1959: Mrs Margaret Thatcher</li>
<li>1959–1970: Mrs Margaret Thatcher <span class="noexcerpt" style="font-size:85%;"><a href="/wiki/Member_of_the_British_House_of_Commons" class="mw-redirect" title="Member of the British House of Commons">MP</a></span></li>
<li>1970–1979: <a href="/wiki/Privy_Council_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Privy Council of the United Kingdom">The Rt Hon</a> Margaret Thatcher <span class="noexcerpt" style="font-size:85%;"><a href="/wiki/Member_of_the_British_House_of_Commons" class="mw-redirect" title="Member of the British House of Commons">MP</a></span></li>
<li>1979–1980: The Rt Hon Margaret Thatcher <span class="noexcerpt" style="font-size:85%;"><a href="/wiki/Member_of_the_British_House_of_Commons" class="mw-redirect" title="Member of the British House of Commons">MP</a>&#32;<a href="/wiki/Honorary_Fellow_of_the_Royal_Institute_of_Chemistry" class="mw-redirect" title="Honorary Fellow of the Royal Institute of Chemistry">HonFRIC</a></span></li>
<li>1980–1983: The Rt Hon Margaret Thatcher <span class="noexcerpt" style="font-size:85%;"><a href="/wiki/Member_of_the_British_House_of_Commons" class="mw-redirect" title="Member of the British House of Commons">MP</a>&#32;<a href="/wiki/Honorary_Fellows_of_the_Royal_Society_of_Chemistry" title="Honorary Fellows of the Royal Society of Chemistry">HonFRSC</a></span></li>
<li>1983–1990: The Rt Hon Margaret Thatcher <span class="noexcerpt" style="font-size:85%;"><a href="/wiki/Member_of_the_British_House_of_Commons" class="mw-redirect" title="Member of the British House of Commons">MP</a>&#32;<a href="/wiki/Fellow_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Fellow of the Royal Society">FRS</a>&#32;<a href="/wiki/Honorary_Fellows_of_the_Royal_Society_of_Chemistry" title="Honorary Fellows of the Royal Society of Chemistry">HonFRSC</a></span></li>
<li>1990–1991: The Rt Hon Margaret Thatcher <span class="noexcerpt" style="font-size:85%;"><a href="/wiki/Member_of_the_Order_of_Merit" class="mw-redirect" title="Member of the Order of Merit">OM</a>&#32;<a href="/wiki/Member_of_the_British_House_of_Commons" class="mw-redirect" title="Member of the British House of Commons">MP</a>&#32;<a href="/wiki/Fellow_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Fellow of the Royal Society">FRS</a>&#32;<a href="/wiki/Honorary_Fellows_of_the_Royal_Society_of_Chemistry" title="Honorary Fellows of the Royal Society of Chemistry">HonFRSC</a></span></li>
<li>1991–1992: The Rt Hon Margaret Thatcher <span class="noexcerpt" style="font-size:85%;"><a href="/wiki/Member_of_the_Order_of_Merit" class="mw-redirect" title="Member of the Order of Merit">OM</a>&#32;<a href="/wiki/Dame_of_Justice_of_the_Order_of_St_John" class="mw-redirect" title="Dame of Justice of the Order of St John">DStJ</a>&#32;<a href="/wiki/Member_of_the_British_House_of_Commons" class="mw-redirect" title="Member of the British House of Commons">MP</a>&#32;<a href="/wiki/Fellow_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Fellow of the Royal Society">FRS</a>&#32;<a href="/wiki/Honorary_Fellows_of_the_Royal_Society_of_Chemistry" title="Honorary Fellows of the Royal Society of Chemistry">HonFRSC</a></span></li>
<li>1992: The Rt Hon Margaret Thatcher <span class="noexcerpt" style="font-size:85%;"><a href="/wiki/Member_of_the_Order_of_Merit" class="mw-redirect" title="Member of the Order of Merit">OM</a>&#32;<a href="/wiki/Dame_of_Justice_of_the_Order_of_St_John" class="mw-redirect" title="Dame of Justice of the Order of St John">DStJ</a>&#32;<a href="/wiki/Fellow_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Fellow of the Royal Society">FRS</a>&#32;<a href="/wiki/Honorary_Fellows_of_the_Royal_Society_of_Chemistry" title="Honorary Fellows of the Royal Society of Chemistry">HonFRSC</a></span></li>
<li>1992–1995: The Rt Hon The Baroness Thatcher <span class="noexcerpt" style="font-size:85%;"><a href="/wiki/Member_of_the_Order_of_Merit" class="mw-redirect" title="Member of the Order of Merit">OM</a>&#32;<a href="/wiki/Dame_of_Justice_of_the_Order_of_St_John" class="mw-redirect" title="Dame of Justice of the Order of St John">DStJ</a>&#32;<a href="/wiki/Privy_Council_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Privy Council of the United Kingdom">PC</a>&#32;<a href="/wiki/Fellow_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Fellow of the Royal Society">FRS</a>&#32;<a href="/wiki/Honorary_Fellows_of_the_Royal_Society_of_Chemistry" title="Honorary Fellows of the Royal Society of Chemistry">HonFRSC</a></span></li>
<li>1995–2013: The Rt Hon The Baroness Thatcher <span class="noexcerpt" style="font-size:85%;"><a href="/wiki/Lady_of_the_Order_of_the_Garter" class="mw-redirect" title="Lady of the Order of the Garter">LG</a>&#32;<a href="/wiki/Member_of_the_Order_of_Merit" class="mw-redirect" title="Member of the Order of Merit">OM</a>&#32;<a href="/wiki/Dame_of_Justice_of_the_Order_of_St_John" class="mw-redirect" title="Dame of Justice of the Order of St John">DStJ</a>&#32;<a href="/wiki/Privy_Council_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Privy Council of the United Kingdom">PC</a>&#32;<a href="/wiki/Fellow_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Fellow of the Royal Society">FRS</a>&#32;<a href="/wiki/Honorary_Fellows_of_the_Royal_Society_of_Chemistry" title="Honorary Fellows of the Royal Society of Chemistry">HonFRSC</a></span></li></ul>
</div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h3>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In her foreword to the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Conservative_Party_(UK)_general_election_manifestos" title="List of Conservative Party (UK) general election manifestos">Conservative manifesto</a> of 1979, Thatcher wrote of "a feeling of helplessness, that we are a once great nation that has somehow fallen behind".<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-7">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Margaret and her elder sister Muriel were raised in the bottom of two flats owned by their parents on North Parade.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett20063_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett20063-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-90"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-90">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The hang-up has always been the voice. Not the timbre so much as, well, the <em>tone</em>&#160;&#8211;&#32;the condescending explanatory whine which treats the squirming interlocutor as an eight-year-old child with personality deficiencies. It has been fascinating, recently, to watch her striving to eliminate this. BBC2 <i>News Extra</i> on Tuesday night rolled a clip from May 1973 demonstrating the Thatcher sneer at full pitch. (She was saying that she wouldn't <em>dream</em> of seeking the leadership.) She sounded like a cat sliding down a blackboard."<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJames1977119–120_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJames1977119–120-89">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-95"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-95">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Thatcher succeeded in completely suppressing her Lincolnshire dialect except when under stress, notably after provocation from <a href="/wiki/Denis_Healey" title="Denis Healey">Denis Healey</a> in the Commons in 1983, when she accused the <a href="/wiki/Shadow_Cabinet_of_Michael_Foot" title="Shadow Cabinet of Michael Foot">Labour frontbench</a> of being <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/frit#Etymology_2" class="extiw" title="wikt:frit"><i>frit</i></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-227"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-227">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Thatcher spoke to the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">Commons</a> on the day of the bombing: "The United States has more than 330,000&#160;members of her forces in Europe to defend our liberty. Because they are here, they are subject to terrorist attack. It is inconceivable that they should be refused the right to use American aircraft and American pilots in the inherent right of self-defence, to defend their own people."<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-347"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-347">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Nigel_Lawson" title="Nigel Lawson">Nigel Lawson</a> listed the Thatcherite ideals as "<a href="/wiki/Free_market" title="Free market">free markets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fiscal_conservatism" title="Fiscal conservatism">financial discipline</a>, firm control over <a href="/wiki/Public_expenditure" title="Public expenditure">public expenditure</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tax_cut" title="Tax cut">tax cuts</a>, nationalism, '<a href="/wiki/Victorian_values" class="mw-redirect" title="Victorian values">Victorian values</a>' (of the <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Smiles" title="Samuel Smiles">Samuel Smiles</a> <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/heaven_helps_those_who_help_themselves" class="extiw" title="wikt:heaven helps those who help themselves">self-help variety</a>), privatisation and a dash of <a href="/wiki/Populism" title="Populism">populism</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELawson199264_346-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELawson199264-346">&#91;341&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-350"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-350">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Statement issued 10 July 1988: "All too often the ills of this country are passed off as those of society. Similarly, when action is required, society is called upon to act. But society as such does not exist except as a concept. Society is made up of people. It is people who have duties and beliefs and resolve. It is people who get things done. She prefers to think in terms of the acts of individuals and families as the real sinews of society rather than of society as an abstract concept. Her approach to society reflects her fundamental belief in personal responsibility and choice. To leave things to <em>society</em> is to run away from the real decisions, practical responsibility and effective action."<sup id="cite_ref-society_349-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-society-349">&#91;343&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div>

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore201387-341"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore201387_341-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFMoore2013">Moore (2013)</a>, p.&#160;87: "Neither at the beginning of her career nor when she was prime minister, did Margaret Thatcher ever reject the wartime foundations of the welfare state, whether in health, social policy or education. In this she was less radical than her critics or some of her admirers supposed. Her concern was to focus more on abuse of the system, on bureaucracy and union militancy, and on the growth of what later came to be called the dependency culture, rather than on the system itself."</span>
</li>
